رجوع پر دو گواہ بنانا مستحب ہے اور بغیر گواہوں کے بھی رجوع درست ہے، اقوال فقہاء
قال ويستحب ان يشهد على الرجعة شاهدين فان لم يشهد صحت الرجعة وقال الشافعي في أحد قوليه لا يصح وهو قول مالك لقوله تعالى واشهدو اذوی عدل منكم والامر للايجاب ولنا اطلاق النصوص عن قيد الاشهاد ولانه استدامة للنكاح والشهادة ليست شرطافيه فى حالة البقاء كمافي الفي في الايلاء الا انها تستحب لزيادة الاحتياط كيلا يجرى التناكر فيها وماتلاه محمول عليه الا ترى انه قرنها بالمفارقة وهو فيها مستحب ويستحب ان يعلمها كيلا تقع في المعصية
ترجمہ ( قدوری) نے کہا کہ مستحب ہے کہ رجعت پر دو گواہ بنالے۔ پس اگر گواہ نہ بنایا تو بھی رجعت صحیح ہے اور امام شافعی نے اپنے دو قولوں میں سے ایک میں فرمایا کہ میچ نہیں ہے اور یہ قول ہے امام مالک کا باری تعالیٰ کے قول و اشهدوا ذوى عدل منكم کی وجہ ہے۔ یعنی تم لوگ اپنے میں سے دو عادل گواہ کر لو اور امر ایجاب کیلئے ہے اور ہماری دلیل قید الشہادۃ سے نصوص کا مطلق ہونا ہے اور اسلئے کہ رجعت بقاء نکاح کا نام ہے اور نکاح میں حالت بقاء میں شہادت شرط نہیں ہے۔ جیسے ایلاء میں رجوع کرنے میں (گواہ کرنے شرط نہیں ) مگر یہ کہ شہادت مستحب ہے۔ زیادتی احتیاط کی وجہ سے تا کہ رجعت میں انکار جاری نہ ہو سکے اور امام شافعی نے جو آیت تلاوت کی ہے وہ اس پر محمول ہے۔ کیا نہیں دیکھتا تو کہ شہادت کو مفارقت کے ساتھ ملا دیا ہے۔ حالانکہ مفارقت میں گواہ بنانا مستحب ہے اور مستحب ہے کہ عورت کو آگاہ کر دے۔ تا کہ وہ معصیت میں نہ پڑ جائے۔ تشریح صاحب قدوری نے بیان فرمایا کہ ہمارے نزدیک رجعت پر شاہدین کو گواہ بنانا مستحب ہے۔ یعنی دو مسلمان مردوں سے کہے کہ تم گواہ رہو میں نے اپنی بیوی سے مراجعت کر لی ہے اور اگر گواہ نہیں بنایا ہے تو بھی رجعت صحیح ہے۔ اور امام شافعی کا ایک قول یہ ہے کہ بغیر شہادت شاہدین کے رجعت صحیح نہیں ہے، اور یہی قول ہے امام مالک کا۔ صاحب فتح القدیر نے بیان کیا ہے کہ مالکیہ کی کتابوں میں مذکور ہے کہ بغیر شہادت شاہدین کے رجعت صحیح ہے۔ البتہ گواہ بنانا مندوب ہے۔ ایسا ہی شرح طحاوی میں ہے۔ پس صاحب ہدایہ کا ذکر کردہ قول امام مالک کی ایک روایت ہوگی۔ حاصل یہ کہ امام شافعی کی ایک روایت اور امام مالک کی ایک روایت کے مطابق رجعت میں شہادت واجب ہے۔ مگر اس روایت کے مطابق امام مالک پر تعجب ہے کہ نکاح میں شہادت کو شرط قرار نہیں دیا اور رجعت پر شہادت کو شرط قرار دیا ہے۔
(معینی شرح کنز، فتح القدير مع الكلابه )
ان دونوں حضرات کی طرف سے وجوب رجعت پر دلیل آیت ہے فاذا بلغن اجلهن فامسكوهن بمعروف او فارقوهن بمعروف و اشهدوا ذوى عدل منکم یعنی جب ان کی عدت کا وقت قریب ختم ہو جائے تو ان کو روک لو، بغیر ضرر پہنچائے یا ان کو جدا کردو، بغیر ضرر پہنچائے اور اپنے میں سے دو عادل گواہوں کو شاہد بنا لو آیت میں اشهدوا صیغہ امر ہے اور امر کا موجب وجوب ہے۔ اس وجہ سے رجعت میں گواہ بنانا واجب ہے۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ رجعت کے سلسلہ میں جو نصوص منقول ہیں وہ تمام قیدا شہاد سے مطلق ہیں ۔ مثلا
Making two witnesses on the return is mustahab, and even without witnesses the return is valid; quotes of jurists. He (al-Quduri) said: It is recommended to have two witnesses for the raj'ah, and if he does not have witnesses, the raj'ah is valid. Al-Shafi'i said in one of his two sayings: it is not valid, and this is the saying of Malik, because of the saying of Allah: 'And call to witness two just men among you' (65:2), and the command is for obligation. And our proof is the absoluteness of the texts from the restriction of witnessing, and because it is a continuation of marriage, and witnessing is not a condition for it in the state of continuity, as in the case of ila' (vow of abstinence from intercourse), except that it is recommended for extra precaution so that denial may not occur therein, and what follows is carried upon that. Do you not see that He linked it with separation, and therein witnessing is recommended? And it is recommended to inform her so that she may not fall into sin. Translation: Al-Quduri said: It is recommended to have two witnesses for the raj'ah. So if he does not have witnesses, the raj'ah is still valid. And Imam al-Shafi'i said in one of his two sayings that it is not valid, and this is the saying of Imam Malik, because of the saying of Allah: 'And call to witness two just men among you', meaning you should have two just witnesses from among you, and the command indicates obligation. And our proof is the absoluteness of the texts from the restriction of witnessing, and because raj'ah is called the continuation of marriage, and witnessing is not a condition for it in the state of continuity, as in the case of ila' (vow of abstinence from intercourse) where witnessing is not a condition when returning from it, except that witnessing is recommended for extra precaution so that denial may not occur therein. And the verse that Imam al-Shafi'i recited is carried upon that. Do you not see that He linked witnessing with separation, while in separation, making witnesses is recommended? And it is recommended to inform her so that she may not fall into sin. Explanation: Al-Quduri stated that according to us, having two witnesses for raj'ah is recommended. That is, he says to two Muslim men: 'Be witnesses that I have returned to my wife.' And if he did not have witnesses, the raj'ah is still valid. And Imam al-Shafi'i has one saying that without the testimony of witnesses, the raj'ah is not valid, and this is the saying of Imam Malik. The author of Fath al-Qadir stated that in the books of the Malikis it is mentioned that without the testimony of witnesses, the raj'ah is valid, but making witnesses is recommended (mandub). The same is in Sharh al-Tahawi. So the saying of Imam Malik mentioned by the author of al-Hidayah would be one narration. The conclusion is that according to one narration of Imam al-Shafi'i and one narration of Imam Malik, witnessing is obligatory in raj'ah. But according to this narration, it is surprising regarding Imam Malik that he did not make witnessing a condition in marriage but made it a condition in raj'ah. (Mu'ini Sharh al-Kanz, Fath al-Qadir with al-Kilabah). From these two persons, the proof for the obligation of witnessing in raj'ah is the verse: 'When they have reached their term, retain them in kindness or separate from them in kindness, and call to witness two just men among you.' That is, when their 'iddah time is about to end, either retain them without harm, or separate them without harm, and have two just witnesses from among you. In the verse, 'ashhidoo' is a command form, and a command necessitates obligation. For this reason, making witnesses in raj'ah is obligatory. Our proof is that the texts transmitted concerning raj'ah are all absolute from the restriction of witnessing. For example...
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning whether witnesses are required for the validity of raj'ah (revocable return after divorce). The base text (matn) of al-Quduri states that it is mustahab (recommended) to have two witnesses, but if they are not present, the raj'ah remains valid. This is the Hanafi position. The passage then presents the opposing view of Imam al-Shafi'i in one of his sayings and a narration from Imam Malik that witnesses are wajib (obligatory) for validity. Their evidence is the Quranic verse 65:2, which commands 'And call to witness two just men among you.' The Hanafi response is twofold: first, the texts on raj'ah are absolute and do not restrict it to the presence of witnesses; second, raj'ah is a continuation of marriage, and witnesses are not a condition in the state of continuity (like in ila' where returning from the vow does not require witnesses). The command in the verse is interpreted as recommendation (madd al-darurah) because it is linked with the recommended acts of retaining or separating. The passage further notes that according to the author of Fath al-Qadir, the Maliki school actually holds that raj'ah is valid without witnesses, but it is recommended to have them, so the disagreement may be based on different Maliki narrations. Ultimately, the Hanafi position is that witnessing is mustahab, not wajib, and the raj'ah is valid without it.

Key Terms
إشهاد (ishhad): The act of having witnesses; specifically, having two just Muslim men witness the declaration of raj'ah.
رجعة (raj'ah): Revocable return to the wife during the 'iddah period after a revocable divorce.
مستحب (mustahab): Recommended; an action that is rewarded if done but not punished if omitted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the ruling on having witnesses for raj'ah (revocable return)?
A) Wajib (obligatory) – without witnesses, raj'ah is invalid
B) Mustahab (recommended) – raj'ah is valid even without witnesses
C) Makruh tahrimi – witnesses should not be appointed
D) Mubah – witnesses are optional but have no reward

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that witnesses for raj'ah are mustahab (recommended) but not wajib, and the raj'ah is valid without them.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
6 min
Witnesses for Raj'a: Recommended but Not Required
Hanafi view on necessity of witnesses for valid revocation of divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on witnesses for raj'a, apply the Hanafi textual and rational proofs, and evaluate the opposing Shafi'i/Maliki argument.

Core Idea
Witnesses for raj'a are recommended, not obligatory; the act remains valid without them.

In Islamic family law, a husband can revoke a revocable divorce (raj'a) during the wife's iddah. A key difference between schools is whether this revocation requires witnesses. The Hanafi school holds that witnesses are recommended (mustahabb) but not obligatory, while the Shafi'i and Maliki schools, in some narrations, require them. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi proofs from the Quran, Sunnah, and legal reasoning, and contrasts them with the opposing view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling on witnesses for raj'a as presented by Al-Quduri in the matn of Al-Hidayah. Note the precise hukm level: mustahabb (recommended), not wajib.
The text explicitly uses 'yustaḥabb'.
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
The Shafi'i school (in one of two opinions) and the Maliki school (in one narration) argue that witnesses are wajib. Their primary evidence is the Quranic verse 65:2: 'wa-ashhidū dhawī ʿadlin minkum' – the imperative indicates obligation.
This is the core of the ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze the Hanafi textual proof (dalīl naqlī)
The Hanafi school responds that the various Quranic and hadith texts on raj'a are absolute (muṭlaq) and do not mention witnesses. Adding such a restriction is impermissible. Examples: 'fa-amsikūhunna bi-maʿrūf', 'wa-buʿūlatuhunna aḥaqqu biraddihinna', etc.
Absolute texts cannot be restricted without another clear proof.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rational proof (dalīl ʿaqlī) and interpretation of the verse
Raj'a is a continuation of marriage, and witnessing is not required for its continuation, as seen in īlāʾ revocation. The verse 'wa-ashhidū' is paired with separation (mufāraqa), where witnessing is unanimously recommended, implying it is also recommended for retention. Thus the imperative is interpreted as recommendation.
This refutes the opponents' claim of obligation.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf) and muftā bihī
The practical difference: If a husband revokes without witnesses, the Hanafi school deems the raj'a valid; the opposing school deems it invalid. The Hanafi position is the relied-upon view (muftā bihī).
Also note: it is recommended to inform the wife to prevent her from unknowingly committing sin.

Chapter: Divorce of the Sick. Ashraf al-Hidaya Urdu Commentary on Hidaya Volume 5. It is recommended to take two witnesses for revocation (raj'a). If he does not take witnesses, the revocation is valid. Al-Shafi'i said in one of his two opinions: it is not valid, and this is Malik's view, due to the verse 'And bring to witness two just men from among you' (Quran 65:2), and the command (amr) indicates obligation. Our proof: the texts (nusus) are absolute (mutlaq) without the restriction of witnessing, and because revocation is a continuation of marriage, and witnessing is not a condition in the state of continuation, as in the case of revoking in ila' (vow of abstinence). However, it is recommended for extra caution so that denial does not occur. And the verse cited by al-Shafi'i is interpreted accordingly; do you not see that he paired witnessing with separation (mufaraqa), where witnessing is recommended? And it is recommended to inform her so that she does not fall into sin. Translation: Quduri said: It is recommended to take two witnesses for revocation; if no witnesses are taken, revocation is valid. Al-Shafi'i in one of his two opinions said it is not valid, and this is Malik's view, due to the verse 'And bring to witness two just men from among you' (Quran 65:2). The command indicates obligation. Our proof: the texts are absolute without the restriction of witnessing, and because revocation is a continuation of marriage, witnessing is not a condition in the state of continuation, as in ila' where witnessing is not a condition. But witnessing is recommended for extra caution so that denial does not occur. The verse cited by al-Shafi'i is interpreted upon recommendation. Do you not see that he paired witnessing with separation, whereas in separation witnessing is recommended? And it is recommended to inform her so that she does not fall into sin. Explanation: Quduri stated that in our view, taking witnesses for revocation is recommended—i.e., he says to two Muslim men, 'Bear witness that I have revoked my wife.' If he does not take witnesses, revocation is still valid. Al-Shafi'i's one opinion is that without witnesses revocation is not valid, and this is Malik's view. Fath al-Qadir mentioned that in Maliki books it is stated that revocation without witnesses is valid, but taking witnesses is recommended (mandub). Similarly in Sharh al-Tahawi. Thus the opinion attributed to Malik by al-Hidaya may be one narration. The result: according to one narration from al-Shafi'i and one from Malik, witnessing in revocation is obligatory (wajib). But according to this narration, Malik's position is surprising because witnessing is not a condition in marriage but is a condition in revocation. (Ma'ini Sharh al-Kanz, Fath al-Qadir ma'a al-kalaba [؟]) Both of them present the verse as evidence: 'When they have nearly reached their term, then retain them in a good manner or separate from them in a good manner, and bring to witness two just men from among you' (Quran 65:2). The word 'ashhidu' is an imperative, and the imperative indicates obligation. Therefore, taking witnesses in revocation is obligatory. Our proof: the texts concerning revocation are all absolute without the restriction of witnessing, e.g., 'Retain them in a good manner' (Quran 65:2), 'Divorce is twice, then retention in a good manner' (Quran 2:229), 'Their husbands have more right to take them back' (Quran 2:228), 'There is no sin upon them if they reunite' (Quran 2:232), and the Prophet's saying: 'Order your son to take her back.' If witnessing were made obligatory in revocation, it would necessitate adding to the absolute texts, which is impermissible. Rational proof: revocation is a continuation of marriage, and witnessing is not a condition for the continuation of marriage. Therefore, witnessing is also not a condition for revocation, just as witnessing is not a condition for revoking in ila', because that is also a state of continuation of marriage. However, witnessing is recommended for extra caution so that after the iddah expires, people do not spread rumors due to ignorance that so-and-so divorced his wife and then kept her after the iddah. As for the verse presented by al-Shafi'i, it is also interpreted as recommendation, because Allah paired witnessing with separation (mufaraqa). He said: 'Or separate them in a good manner, and bring to witness two just men from among you' (Quran 65:2), and in separation, witnessing is unanimously recommended. Therefore, witnessing in revocation and retention should also be recommended. The author of al-Hidaya said: It is also recommended to inform the wife of the revocation so that she does not fall into sin. This is because the wife might think that her husband has not revoked her and she remarries after the iddah, and then she might engage in sexual relations with the second husband, which would make her sinful. However, even if the wife is not informed, the revocation is still valid. If the wife's iddah has expired and the husband says, 'I revoked you during the iddah,' and the wife affirms him, then it is a valid revocation. If she denies, then the wife's word is taken as authoritative (qawl qawlha), because the husband is informing of something he cannot create at present, so he is accused (muttafam). Only by her affirmation is the accusation removed. According to Abu Hanifa, there is no oath upon her. This is the issue of istihlaf (taking an oath) in the six matters, and it has passed in the Book of Nikah. Translation: If the iddah has expired and the man says, 'I revoked her during the iddah,' and the wife affirms him, then it is a revocation. If she denies, then her word is taken as authoritative, because the husband is informing of something he cannot now possess; if he wanted to create it now, he would be incapable. But by her affirmation, the accusation is removed. According to Abu Hanifa, there is no oath upon her. This is the issue of istihlaf in the six matters, and it has passed in the Book of Nikah. Explanation: The issue is that after the iddah expires, the husband says to his wife, 'I revoked you during the iddah.' Then the wife...
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the necessity of witnesses (shuhūd) for the revocation of a revocable divorce (rajʿa). The Hanafi position, stated by Al-Quduri and expanded upon by the commentator, is that taking two Muslim male witnesses is recommended (mustaḥabb) but not obligatory. If a husband revokes without witnesses, the rajʿa is still legally valid. The Shāfiʿī school, in one of its two opinions, and the Mālikī school in one narration hold that witnesses are obligatory (wājib) based on the Qurʾānic verse: 'And bring to witness two just men from among you' (65:2), interpreting the imperative form (amr) as indicating obligation. The Hanafi response uses two arguments: First, naqlī (textual): The various Qurʾānic verses and Prophetic traditions about rajʿa are absolute (muṭlaq) and do not mention witnesses; requiring witnesses would add a restriction not found in the texts, which is impermissible. Second, ʿaqlī (rational): Rajʿa is a continuation of marriage (istidāma al-nikāḥ), and at the time of marriage’s inception, witnesses are required, but for its continuation, they are not. An analogy is made with revocation of ʾīlāʾ (vow of abstinence), where witnesses are not required. The verse cited by the opponents is interpreted as referring to separation (mufāraqa), where witnesses are unanimously recommended, not obligatory. Additionally, the text discusses that it is recommended to inform the wife of the rajʿa to prevent her from unknowingly committing sin by remarrying. Finally, a scenario is presented: if the ʿiddah has expired and the husband claims he revoked during it, the wife’s affirmation makes the rajʿa valid; if she denies, her word is taken as authoritative because the husband is considered a claimant unable to produce evidence, and no oath is required upon her according to Abū Ḥanīfa. This ruling is from the category of istiḥlāf in six specific matters.

Key Terms
رجعة (rajʿa): The revocation of a revocable divorce (ṭalāq rajʿī) by the husband during the wife's ʿiddah period, returning her to the marital bond without a new contract.
شهادة (shahāda): Testimony or witnessing; here it refers to the presence of two male Muslim witnesses to the act of revocation.
مستحب (mustaḥabb): A recommended act; doing it brings reward, but omitting it does not incur sin nor affect validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on having witnesses for a valid raj'a (revocation of divorce)?
A) Obligatory (wājib)
B) Recommended (mustaḥabb)
C) Not recommended (makrūh)
D) Forbidden (ḥarām)

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, witnesses for raj'a are recommended for caution, not required for validity.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
6 min
Witnesses for Revocation (Ruju') are Mustahabb not Wajib
Refuting Shafi'i claim that witnesses are obligatory in ruju'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school considers witnesses for ruju' as mustahabb and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on the Quranic verse.

Core Idea
Witnesses in ruju' are mustahabb—not wajib—because ruju' is a mere continuation of marriage, which does not require testimony.

This passage presents the Hanafi refutation of the Shafi'i claim that witnesses are obligatory (wajib) for ruju' (revocation of divorce). The core of the dispute lies in interpreting the Quranic command in verse 65:2. The Hanafi school argues that requiring witnesses would add an impermissible condition to the absolute texts, and that rational analogy to the nature of ruju' as a continuation of marriage supports recommendation, not obligation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafi school holds witnesses for ruju' as mustahabb, not wajib. The ruju' is valid without witnesses.
This is the foundational ruling to understand.
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that witnesses are wajib, citing the imperative in Quran 65:2: 'and call to witness two just men among you.'
The imperative form (amr) is the basis of the Shafi'i argument.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the proof
The Hanafi school interprets the imperative as indicating istihbab, not wujub, because the verse pairs witnessing with separation (mufaraqah), where witnesses are unanimously mustahabb. Thus the same ruling applies to ruju'.
Contextual evidence (qarina) diverts the imperative from obligation to recommendation.
4
Examine the rational proof (dalil aqli)
Ruju' is continuation of marriage; continuation does not require witnesses, analogous to returning from ila'.
This analogy strengthens the Hanafi position.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
The practical consequence: under Hanafi law, ruju' is valid without witnesses; under Shafi'i law, it would be invalid without them.
This highlights the real-world impact of the ikhtilaf.

اور رہی وہ آیت جس کو امام شافعی نے پیش کیا ہے سو وہ بھی استحباب پر محمول ہے اور دلیل یہ ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ نے شہادت کو مفارقت کے ساتھ ملا کر ذکر کیا ہے۔ چنانچہ فرمایا ہے او فارقوهن بمعروف و اشهدوا ذوى عدل منکم اور مفارقت میں شہادت بالا تفاق مستحب ہے۔ اس وجہ سے رجعت اور امساک میں بھی شہادت مستحب ہونی چاہئے۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا ہے کہ عورت کو رجعت کی اطلاع کرنا بھی مستحب ہے تا کہ وہ معصیت میں نہ پڑ جائے ۔ کیونکہ عورت کبھی یہ سمجھ کر کہ میرے شوہر نے رجعت نہیں کی اور عدت گذرگئی دوسری شادی کر لیتی ہے اور دوسرے شوہر سے وطی بھی کر سکتی ہے تو اس صورت میں یہ عورت گنہگار ہوگی۔ لیکن اس کے باوجود اگر عورت کو رجعت سے آگاہ نہیں کیا ہے تو بھی رجعت صحیح ہو جائے گی۔
If making witnesses obligatory in ruju' were required, it would necessitate adding to the absolute texts, which is impermissible. The rational proof is that ruju' is the continuation of marriage, and for the continuation of marriage, witnesses are not a condition. Therefore, witnesses are not a condition for ruju' either. Just as for returning from ila', witnesses are not a condition because that too is a state of continuation of marriage. However, due to extra precaution, witnesses in ruju' are mustahabb. So that after the 'iddah passes, due to ignorance, people do not spread evil talk that so-and-so divorced his wife and after the 'iddah passed kept her with him. As for the verse that Imam Shafi'i presented, it is also interpreted as mustahabb, and the proof is that Allah mentioned witnesses together with separation. Thus He said: 'Or separate from them with kindness and call to witness two just men among you.' In separation, witnesses are agreed to be mustahabb. Therefore, in ruju' and retention, witnesses should also be mustahabb. The author of Hidayah said that informing the wife of ruju' is also mustahabb so that she does not fall into sin. Because sometimes a woman, thinking that her husband did not revoke and the 'iddah has passed, marries another and may have intercourse with the second husband; in that case she would be sinful. But despite this, if the woman is not informed of the ruju', the ruju' will still be valid.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the requirement of witnesses for ruju' (revocation of divorce). The Hanafi position holds that witnesses are mustahabb (recommended), not wajib (obligatory). The proof is twofold: first, a rational proof (dalil aqli) that ruju' is a continuation of the marriage, and the continuation of marriage does not require witnesses, analogous to returning from ila'. Second, the verse cited by Imam Shafi'i (Quran 65:2) is interpreted as indicating istihbab, not wujub, because the context is separation (mufaraqah), where witnesses are agreed to be mustahabb; thus in ruju' and retention (imsak) they are also mustahabb. The passage also notes that informing the wife of ruju' is mustahabb to prevent her from unknowingly committing sin by remarrying, but even without notification, the ruju' is valid. This follows the Hanafi principle that conditions not stated in the original texts cannot be added.

Key Terms
رجعت (ruju'): Revocation of divorce within the 'iddah period; the husband's act of taking back his wife without a new marriage contract.
شہادت (shahadah): Testimony of witnesses; in this context, the presence of two just male witnesses at the time of ruju'.
استحباب (istihbab): Recommendaion; an act that is not obligatory but meritorious.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on having witnesses for ruju' (revocation of divorce)?
A) Wajib (obligatory) - without witnesses ruju' is invalid
B) Mustahabb (recommended) - witnesses are not required but encouraged
C) Haram (forbidden) - witnesses should not be present
D) Mubah (permissible) - optional with no preference

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, witnesses for ruju' are mustahabb, not wajib, because ruju' is a continuation of marriage and the Quranic imperative is interpreted as recommendation when paired with separation.

Wife's Confirmation or Denial of Ruju' After 'Iddah
Ruling on husband's claim of ruju' after 'iddah with wife's response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a husband's claim of ruju' after 'iddah and apply the condition of the wife's confirmation or denial.

Core Idea
A husband's unproven claim of ruju' after 'iddah is accepted only if the wife confirms it; otherwise, her word prevails.

This passage addresses a delicate situation: after the 'iddah has expired, a husband claims he revoked his wife during the 'iddah, but there is no evidence. The Hanafi school gives decisive weight to the wife's response. Her confirmation validates the ruju', while her denial nullifies it, based on the principle that the husband is accused of a past act he cannot prove. We will examine the ruling, its rational proof, and the related principle of no oath in six matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and parties
Recognize that the 'iddah has expired, the husband makes a claim about a past ruju', and the wife either confirms or denies.
The claim is about an event during the 'iddah, but stated after its expiration.
2
State the ruling for each response
If the wife confirms, the ruju' is valid. If she denies, her word is accepted and no ruju' occurs.
The ruling hinges entirely on the wife's statement.
3
Understand the rational proof (dalil aqli)
The husband is informing about something he cannot produce at the present moment (i.e., proof of the past act), so he is considered an accuser (muttaham).
Because he cannot establish his claim, his word alone is not credible.
4
Recognize the exception: wife's confirmation removes accusation
When the wife confirms, the accusation (tuhmah) is lifted, and the ruju' is established.
Confirmation acts as a form of evidence that compensates for the lack of proof.
5
Note the absence of oath according to Abu Hanifa
Imam Abu Hanifa rules that no oath is required from the wife in this scenario, as it is one of six matters where oath is not administered.
This principle is rooted in the Book of Nikah and is a distinguishing Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ترجمہ اور اگر عدت گزرگئی پس مرد نے کہا میں نے اس کی عدت میں رجوع کر لیا تھا۔ پس عورت نے اس کی تصدیق کر دی تو یہ رجعت ہے اور اگر عورت نے اس کی تکذیب کی تو عورت ہی کا قول ( معتبر) ہوگا۔ کیونکہ شوہر نے اس کو ایسی چیز کی خبر دی ہے جس کا وہ فی الحال مالک نہیں ۔ اگر وہ اس کو (پیدا کرنا چاہے تو منہم ہوگا۔ مگر عورت کی تصدیق کرنے سے تہمت مرتفع ہو جائے گی اور امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک عورت پر قسم نہیں ہے اور چھ چیزوں میں یہ مسئلہ استخلاف ہے اور کتاب النکاح میں گذر چکا۔

تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ عدت گزر جانے کے بعد شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا میں تجھ سے عدت میں مراجعت کر چکا ۔ پس عورت نے اس میں اپنے شوہر کی تصدیق کر دی تو یہ رجعت ہے اور اگر عورت نے تکذیب کی تو عورت کا قول معتبر ہو گا ۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ شوہر نے ایسی چیز کی خبر دی ہے جس کو وہ فی الحال پیدا نہیں کر سکتا۔ تو وہ اس میں متہم ہوگا۔ مگر چونکہ عورت کے تصدیق کر دینے سے بہت دور ہو جاتی ہے۔ اسلئے تصدیق کی صورت میں رجعت ثابت ہو جائے گی۔

صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا وقد مرفی کتاب النکاح ۔ حالانکہ کتاب النکاح میں اس مسئلہ کو بیان نہیں کیا گیا ہے ۔ بلکہ دعوی سکوت علی البکر کے مسئلہ میں صرف اتنا کہا ہے فلا يمين عليها عند ابي حنيفه وهي مسئلة الاستحلاف في الاشياء الستة پھر کہا و سیاتیک فی الدعویٰ اور اس طرح کے بیان کو یہ نہیں کہا جاتا کہ یہ مسئلہ گذر چکا ۔ کیونکہ وہاں رجعت کا ذکر تک نہیں آیا ہے۔ واللہ اعلم
And if the 'iddah has expired, and (the husband) said, 'I had revoked you during the 'iddah,' and she confirms him, then it is a valid ruju'. If she denies him, then her word (counts). Because he informed her of something he cannot produce at the present moment, so he is accused. But with her confirmation, the accusation is removed. And according to Abu Hanifa, there is no oath upon her. This is the issue of requiring an oath in the six things, and it has passed in the Book of Nikah. Translation: If the 'iddah expires and the husband says, 'I had revoked you during the 'iddah,' and the woman confirms him, then this is ruju'. If she denies, then her word is accepted. Because the husband informed her of something he cannot produce now. If he wanted to produce it, he would be a liar. But by her confirmation, the accusation is removed. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, no oath is upon her. This is the issue of oath in the six things, and it has passed in the Book of Nikah. Explanation: After the 'iddah expires, the husband tells his wife, 'I had revoked you during the 'iddah.' If the woman confirms him, it is ruju'. If she denies, her word is accepted. The proof is that the husband informed of something he cannot produce now, so he is accused. But since her confirmation removes the accusation, ruju' is established in case of confirmation. The author of Hidayah said that it has passed in the Book of Nikah, whereas that issue was not mentioned in the Book of Nikah. Rather, in the issue of claiming silence of a virgin, only this was said: 'There is no oath upon her according to Abu Hanifa, and this is the issue of requiring an oath in the six things.' Then he said, 'It will come in the chapter of claims.' Such an expression is not called passing, because there was no mention of ruju' there. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling regarding a husband's claim that he revoked his wife during the 'iddah after the 'iddah has already expired. The Hanafi ruling is: if the wife confirms his statement, the ruju' is valid; if she denies, her word is accepted, and the ruju' is not established. The rational proof (dalil aqli) is that the husband is informing about a past event that he cannot prove at present, so he is considered an accuser; his claim is not credible without evidence. The wife's confirmation removes the accusation, establishing the ruju'. Imam Abu Hanifa also rules that the wife is not required to take an oath in this matter, and it is part of an established principle concerning six specific issues (al-ashya' al-sittah) where no oath is administered. The commentary clarifies a discrepancy in the reference to the Book of Nikah in Al-Hidayah: the issue was not directly discussed there, only alluded to. The author notes this and concludes 'Wa Allah A'lam' (And Allah knows best).

Key Terms
تہمت (tuhmah): Accusation; here refers to the husband being suspected of making a false claim.
استخلاف (istihlaf): The process of requiring an oath; here it is the principle that in certain matters (six things) no oath is taken.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The husband's claim is accepted because he is the one who revokes
B) The wife's word is accepted, ruju' is not established
C) Both are required to take an oath
D) The matter is referred to a judge

Key Takeaway
A husband's unproven claim of ruju' after 'iddah succeeds only if the wife confirms it; otherwise, her denial stands and no ruju' occurs, with no oath required from the wife according to Imam Abu Hanifa.

Ruju' When Wife Claims 'Iddah Expired: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ruju' validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on ruju' when the wife claims her 'iddah has expired, and explain the legal reasoning (istishab vs. amina) behind the dispute.

Core Idea
The wife's trustworthiness overrides the presumption of ongoing 'iddah.

This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two principal disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The scenario: a husband says, 'I have revoked you' and the wife immediately responds, 'My 'iddah has expired.' The disagreement hinges on whether the ruju' is valid. Abu Hanifa says no, because the wife's trustworthy report indicates the 'iddah had already ended by the time of his statement. The Sahibayn say yes, because the default presumption (istishab) is that the 'iddah continues until proven otherwise, and the husband's claim preceded the wife's information.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
Understand exactly what is being disputed: the husband claims he made ruju' during the 'iddah, but the wife says her 'iddah has already expired. The key question: is the ruju' valid?
The wife's response is immediate (mutasil).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Imam Abu Hanifa holds the ruju' is invalid. His proof: the wife is trustworthy (amina) regarding her own 'iddah. When she reports expiry, it indicates that the expiry occurred earlier, and the closest time is the moment of the husband's statement.
He prioritizes her testimony over the presumption of continuity.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold the ruju' is valid. Their proof: the principle of istishab (presumption of continuity). The 'iddah is presumed ongoing until definitive evidence (here, the wife's information) proves otherwise. Since the ruju' preceded her report, it coincided with the presumed 'iddah period.
They give weight to the default state.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: if Abu Hanifa's view is taken, the wife may remarry immediately after the 'iddah; if the Sahibayn's view, the ruju' stands and the marriage continues normally.
This affects the woman's ability to remarry and the husband's right to revoke.
5
Note the analogous case of divorce
The text mentions a similar scenario where the husband says 'I divorced you' and the wife responds 'My 'iddah has expired.' Here, the divorce is effective by consensus because the husband's acknowledgment after expiry is sufficient for divorce, but not for ruju'.
This highlights a difference between talaq and ruju' in the effect of acknowledgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ترجمہ اور اگر شوہر نے کہا کہ میں تجھے سے رجعت کر چکا ہوں ۔ پس عورت نے اس کو جواب دیتے ہوئے کہا کہ میری عدت تو گذر گئی ہے۔ تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک رجعت صحیح نہیں ہے اور صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ صحیح ہے۔ کیونکہ رجعت نے عدت کو پا لیا۔ اسلئے کہ بظاہر عدت باقی ہے۔ یہاں تک کہ عورت (عدت گذر جانے کی خبر دے اور رجعت خبر دینے سے سابق ہے اور اسی وجہ سے اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا میں تجھ کو طلاق دے چکا۔ پس عورت نے اس کو جواب دیتے ہوئے کہا کہ میری عدت گذرگئی ہے تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی اور امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ رجعت نے عدت گذرنے کی حالت کو پالیا ہے۔ اس لئے کہ عورت عدت گزارنے کی خبر دینے میں امین ہے۔ پس جب عورت نے خبر دی (عدت گزر جانے کی ) تو دلالت کی کہ گذر جانا پہلے ہو چکا اور انقضاء کے احوال میں سب سے قریب ترین شوہر کے قول کا حال ہے اور طلاق کا مسئلہ مختلف فیہ ہے اور اگر بالا تفاق ہے تو طلاق واقع ہوگی ۔ انقضاء کے بعد اس کے اقرار سے اور مراجعت اقرار سے ثابت نہیں ہوتی ہے۔

تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا میں تجھ سے عدت میں رجعت کر چکا ہوں ۔ عورت نے اپنے شوہر کو متصل) جواب دیتے ہوئے کہا کہ میری تو عدت بھی گذر چکی ہے تو امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک رجعت صحیح نہیں ہوگی ۔ امام شافعی اور امام احمد کا بھی یہی قول ہے اور صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ رجعت صحیح ہو جائے گی ۔ ساحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ رجعت نے عدت کے زمانے کو پالیا ہے۔ کیونکہ استصحاب حال پر عمل کرتے ہوئے بظاہر عدت باقی
And if the husband said, 'I have revoked you,' and she replied, 'My 'iddah has expired,' then according to Abu Hanifa the ruju' is not valid, but the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said it is valid, because it coincided with the 'iddah as it is apparently still ongoing until she informs, and the ruju' preceded her information. For this reason, if he said to her, 'I have divorced you,' and she replied, 'My 'iddah has expired,' the divorce occurs. According to Abu Hanifa, the ruju' coincided with the state of expiry because she is trustworthy in informing about expiry; when she informs, it indicates that the expiry preceded, and its closest time is the moment of the husband's statement. The issue of divorce is disputed. If it were agreed upon, divorce occurs by his acknowledgment after expiry, but reconciliation is not established by acknowledgment. Translation: And if the husband said, 'I have revoked you,' and she replied, 'My 'iddah has expired,' then according to Imam Abu Hanifa the ruju' is not valid, but the two (Imams Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said it is valid. Because the ruju' encountered the 'iddah since apparently the 'iddah is still ongoing until she informs, and the ruju' preceded the information. For this reason, if he said to her, 'I have divorced you,' and she replied, 'My 'iddah has expired,' the divorce takes place. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that the ruju' coincided with the state of expiry because the woman is trustworthy in reporting expiry; when she reports, it indicates that the expiry occurred earlier, and the closest time to that expiry is the state of the husband's speech. The issue of divorce is disputed. If it were agreed upon, divorce occurs by his acknowledgment after expiry, but reconciliation is not established by acknowledgment. Explanation: The scenario is that the husband told his wife, 'I have revoked you during the 'iddah,' and the wife immediately replied, 'My 'iddah has expired.' According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the ruju' is not valid. Imam Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad also hold this view. The two (Imams Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said that the ruju' is valid. Their proof is that the ruju' encountered the period of 'iddah because, acting on the principle of istishab (presumption of continuity), the 'iddah is apparently still ongoing.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two principal disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), concerning a specific scenario: a husband says 'I have revoked you' and the wife immediately responds 'My 'iddah has expired.' According to Abu Hanifa, the ruju' is invalid because the wife's statement, being trustworthy, indicates that the 'iddah had already expired at the moment of the husband's declaration, so the ruju' did not occur within the 'iddah. The Sahibayn argue that the ruju' is valid because the default presumption (istishab al-hal) is that the 'iddah is ongoing until proven otherwise; since the wife's information came after the husband's statement, the ruju' coincided with the 'iddah period. This is similar to a case where the husband says 'I divorced you' and the wife responds 'My 'iddah has expired' – here, the divorce is effective by consensus because the husband's acknowledgment of divorce after the 'iddah suffices, but for ruju', acknowledgment alone is not sufficient. The passage also notes that Imam Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad agree with Abu Hanifa's position.

Key Terms
استصحاب (istishab): Presumption of continuity; here, the assumption that the 'iddah is still ongoing until evidence of expiry is established.
امین (amin): Trustworthy; the wife is considered reliable in reporting the expiry of her own 'iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the dispute about ruju' when the wife claims 'iddah expired, what is the key principle on which the Sahibayn base their argument?
A) The wife's testimony is always conclusive.
B) The husband's claim is always accepted.
C) The presumption of continuity (istishab) that the 'iddah is ongoing.
D) The need for two witnesses.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's relied-upon position (Sahibayn) is that ruju' is valid when husband revokes before wife reports 'iddah expiry, based on istishab.

Validity of Ruj' When Wife Claims Iddah Ended: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ruj' validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on ruj' validity when the wife claims iddah ended, and explain the proofs and distinction from divorce.

Core Idea
The wife is trustworthy about her iddah; her claim of end precedes husband's ruj' claim, making ruj' invalid.

This lesson examines a classical intra-Hanafi dispute: the husband claims he performed ruj' (return to marriage) during the iddah, but the wife asserts her iddah has already ended. Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) differ on the ruling. Understanding their proofs and the underlying principles of istishab (presumption of continuity) and trustworthiness of women (based on Quran 2:228) is essential for grasping how Hanafi jurists resolve conflicts of claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested act and the two positions
The act is ruj' (husband's return to wife during revocable divorce iddah). The wife claims iddah ended before the husband's claim. Abu Hanifa rules ruj' invalid; Sahibayn rule it valid. Determine the legal consequences of each ruling.
2
Examine the Sahibayn's proof: istishab
The Sahibayn argue that the iddah is presumed to continue until proven otherwise (istishab). Since the husband's statement of ruj' was made before the wife's news of end, it coincided with the iddah and is valid. Note that they analogize to divorce: if husband says 'I divorced you' and wife says 'iddah ended,' divorce occurs.
3
Examine Abu Hanifa's proof: wife's trustworthiness
Abu Hanifa relies on Quran 2:228, which makes women trustworthy about what is in their wombs. Since the wife alone knows her iddah, her claim that it ended is accepted. This means the iddah ended before the husband's claim, so ruj' occurred after iddah and is invalid.
4
Analyze the response to the Sahibayn's analogy
Abu Hanifa responds that the divorce case is itself disputed. Even if agreed, there is a key difference: divorce can be established by confession after iddah, but ruj' cannot because it involves accusation (tuhmah) of interfering with the wife's right to remarry. Thus the analogy fails.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa, the marriage remains dissolved and the wife is free to remarry. Under Sahibayn, the ruj' is valid and the marriage continues. Later fatwa practice often follows Sahibayn due to istishab strength.

When the husband says: 'I have returned to you (raj'tu)' and the wife replies: 'My iddah has ended,' the ruj' is not valid according to Abu Hanifa. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it is valid, because it coincided with the iddah as it is apparently remaining until she informs otherwise, and his statement of ruj' preceded her news. Therefore, if he had said to her: 'I divorced you,' and she replied: 'My iddah has ended,' the divorce occurs. For Abu Hanifa, the ruj' coincided with the state of conclusion because she is trustworthy concerning the news of conclusion. When she informed, it indicates the precedence of conclusion, and the nearest of its conditions is the time of the husband's statement. The divorce case is disputed. If it were agreed, then divorce occurs by his confession after conclusion, but ruj' is not established by confession. Then Urdu commentary: Explanation: The husband says to his wife: 'I have returned to you (raj'tu) during iddah.' The wife immediately replies: 'My iddah has ended.' According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the ruj' is not valid. Imam Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad also hold this view. The two companions say the ruj' is valid. Their proof: ruj' coincided with the iddah period because by istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity) the iddah apparently remains until she informs of its end. Thus his statement of ruj' is earlier, and her informing of the end is later. Ruj' during iddah is valid, so we ruled it valid. Since his ruj' statement precedes her news of iddah end, likewise if the husband says 'I divorced you' and she replies 'My iddah ended,' divorce occurs because his divorce statement is prior to her news of iddah end. Abu Hanifa's proof: his ruj' statement found after the iddah had ended. What is in the womb, the one informing about it is trustworthy. Allah says: 'And it is not lawful for them to conceal what Allah has created in their wombs' (2:228). When she informed of the iddah end, this indicates that the end preceded the information. As for how much precedence, the closest condition to the iddah end is the husband's statement, so her news takes precedence over his statement 'I have returned.' Thus it is established that the iddah ended first, ruj' occurred afterwards, and ruj' after iddah is not valid. The phrase 'and the case of divorce' is a response to the companions' analogy. The response: the case of divorce is also disputed like ruj', so analogy is incorrect. If it is granted that it is agreed, then divorce occurs by his confession after iddah, but ruj' is not established by confession because there is accusation of dealing with another's right. Thus there is a difference between the two cases, so analogy with difference is invalid.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The issue: a husband claims he performed ruj' (return to marriage) during the iddah period, but the wife claims the iddah has already ended. Abu Hanifa rules that the ruj' is invalid, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) rule it valid. The Sahibayn argue by istishab (presumption of continuity): the iddah is presumed to continue until proven otherwise, and the husband's statement of ruj' is earlier in time than the wife's news of iddah end. They analogize to divorce: if the husband says 'I divorced you' and wife says 'iddah ended,' divorce occurs because the divorce statement is earlier. Abu Hanifa counters that the wife is trustworthy about her own iddah (based on Quran 2:228), so her claim that it ended is accepted. The ruj' therefore occurs after the iddah, making it invalid. He further distinguishes the divorce case: even if divorce occurs by confession after iddah, ruj' does not because it involves accusation of tampering with another's right (the wife's right to remarry). Thus the analogy fails. This ruling is based on Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
رجعة (ruj'ah): The husband's return to his wife during her iddah after a revocable divorce, which reinstates the marriage without a new contract.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before she can remarry.
استصحاب (istishab): Presumption of continuity of a ruling until evidence of change is established.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the Sahibayn's opinion is stronger.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the wife's word is accepted regarding her iddah, so a husband's claim of ruj' after she asserts the iddah has ended is invalid, though the Sahibayn differ based on istishab.

Whose Word Prevails: Slave-Girl or Master in Ruj' Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whose word is accepted when master confirms husband's ruj' but slave-girl denies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whose word is accepted in a slave-girl's ruj' dispute and explain the talil based on iddah and ownership.

Core Idea
The slave-girl's word about her iddah prevails, but the master's ownership claim overrides once iddah ends.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about a unique scenario: a slave-girl's husband claims he performed ruj' after her iddah ended, the master confirms, but the slave-girl denies. The key question is whose word is accepted — the slave-girl's or her master's? The answer hinges on whether the focus is on the existence of iddah (which the woman knows best) or on the master's ownership of her marriageable right (bud').

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and the Two Positions
The husband of a slave-girl says after her iddah: 'I returned to her.' The master confirms, but the slave-girl denies. According to Abu Hanifa, the slave-girl's word is accepted; according to the Sahibayn, the master's word is accepted.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, so both views are within the school.
2
Trace the Reasoning of Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa argues that ruj' is built on iddah, and the woman is the authority about her own iddah. Since she denies the ruj', she effectively denies the iddah, so her word is accepted.
The previous lesson established that a free woman's word about her iddah is trusted; Abu Hanifa extends this to slave-girls.
3
Trace the Reasoning of the Sahibayn
The Sahibayn argue that the master owns the slave-girl's bud'. His confirmation of ruj' is an exercise of his ownership right, analogous to a confession of marriage, so his word should be accepted.
They view the master's confirmation as a kind of disposition of his property right.
4
Understand the Nuance: Timing Matters
The text adds: if the dispute arises after the iddah has clearly ended, even Abu Hanifa accepts the master's word in the sound opinion, because the master's right to her marriage has become apparent and she cannot nullify it by her denial.
This shows that the difference is limited to cases where the existence of iddah is still debatable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و اذا قال زوج الامة بعد انقضاء عدتها قد كنت راجعتها وصدقه المولى وكذبه الامة فالقول قولها عند ابي حنيفة وقالا القول قول المولى لان بضعها مملوك له فقد اقربما هو خالص حقه للزوج فشابه الاقرار عليها بالنكاح وهو يقول حكم الرجعة يبتنى على العدة والقول فى العدة قولها فكذافيما يبتنى عليها ولو كان على القلب فعندهما القول قول المولى وكذا عنده في الصحيح لانها منقضية العدة في الحال وقد ظهر ملک المتعة للمولى ولا يقبل قولها فى ابطاله بخلاف الوجه الاول لان المولى بالتصديق في الرجعة مقر بقيام العدة عندها ولا يظهر ملكه مع العدة وان قالت قد انقضت عدتى وقال الزوج والمولى لم تنقض عدتک فالقول قولها لانها امينة فى ذلك اذهى العالمة به. باندی کے شوہر نے اس کی عدت گذرنے کے بعد کہا میں رجوع کر چکا تھا مولیٰ نے شوہر کی تصدیق کر دی باندی تکذیب کرتی ہے قول کس کا معتبر ہوگا، اقوال فقہاء.
When the husband of a slave-girl says after the end of her iddah: 'I had returned to her (raj'tu),' and the master confirms him, but the slave-girl denies, then the word is hers (the slave-girl's) according to Abu Hanifa. The two companions say the word is the master's, because her marriageable right (bud') is owned by him; he (the master) has brought forward what is his pure right to the husband, so it resembles a confession of marriage upon her. He (Abu Hanifa) says: the ruling of ruj' is built upon the iddah, and the word concerning iddah is hers, so likewise concerning what is built upon it. If it were the opposite, according to them the word is the master's, and similarly according to him (Abu Hanifa) in the sound opinion, because the iddah is concluded at present and the master's right to enjoyment has become apparent, and her word is not accepted to nullify it. Unlike the first case, because the master by confirming the ruj' is confessing the existence of iddah in her, and his ownership does not appear with iddah. If she says: 'My iddah has ended,' and the husband and master say: 'Your iddah has not ended,' then the word is hers because she is trustworthy in that, as she is the one who knows it. Urdu: The slave-girl's husband after the end of her iddah said: 'I had returned to her.' The master confirmed, but the slave-girl denied. Whose word is accepted? Statements of the jurists.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a slave-girl whose husband claims he performed ruj' after her iddah has ended, the master confirms this, but the slave-girl denies it. Abu Hanifa says the slave-girl's word is accepted (denial), while the Sahibayn say the master's word is accepted (confirmation). Abu Hanifa's reasoning: ruj' is based on iddah, and the woman is the authority on her iddah (as established in the previous lesson), so since the husband claims ruj' during iddah, the master's confirmation contradicts her claim of iddah end. The master's ownership of her marriageable right (bud') is relevant only after iddah; during iddah, her word prevails. However, in the case where everyone agrees the iddah has ended (the first scenario in the passage: after iddah), Abu Hanifa also says the master's word is accepted in the sound opinion, because the master's right to her marriage has clearly emerged. The passage also notes the sub-case: if the wife says iddah ended but husband and master say it hasn't, then the wife's word is accepted by all, as she is trustworthy about her iddah. This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
بضع (bud'): The marriageable right or the woman's sexuality, which in a slave-girl is owned by her master.
مولى (mawla): The master or owner of a slave.
أمة (amah): A female slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the master is a slave owner and cannot be a witness.
B) Because ruj' is built on iddah, and the woman is the authority on her iddah.
C) Because the slave-girl is more truthful than the master.
D) Because the husband's claim is not supported by evidence.

Key Takeaway
In disputes over ruj' for a slave-girl, the Hanafi school balances the woman's authority over her iddah with the master's ownership of her marriageable right, leading to different rulings depending on the timing.

Whose Statement Is Accepted in Revocable Return of a Slave Woman?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on acceptance of statements in slave woman's raj'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the acceptance of a slave woman's statement in disputes over revocable return, and apply the underlying principles of 'iddah trustworthiness and master's ownership to determine the operative ruling.

Core Idea
The woman is the authority on her own 'iddah, but the master's ownership of usufruct after 'iddah can override her statement.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), concerning whose statement is accepted when a husband claims he performed a revocable return (raj'ah) during the 'iddah of his slave wife, but the slave woman denies it while her master confirms it. The ruling hinges on whether the woman's inherent knowledge of her 'iddah overrides the master's property rights over her usufruct. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind each position and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
The case involves three parties: the husband (who claims he performed raj'ah during the 'iddah), the slave woman (who denies it and says her 'iddah has passed), and the master (who confirms the husband's claim). Determine the conflict of interest: the master and husband together against the slave woman's statement.
2
Recognize the default principle
In Hanafi fiqh, a woman's statement about her own 'iddah is trusted, based on the Quranic verse (2:228) that prohibits women from concealing what is in their wombs. Apply this: the slave woman's denial of the raj'ah implies she claims her 'iddah has ended, so her statement should be authoritative.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's objection
The Sahibayn argue that after the 'iddah, the usufruct (manafi' al-bud') of the slave woman belongs exclusively to the master. Therefore, the master's acknowledgment of the raj'ah is an acknowledgment of his own property right, akin to his acknowledgment of a marriage contract, and should be accepted. The master's ownership after 'iddah is a known fact that overrides the woman's claim.
4
Analyze the four scenarios for consistency
The passage outlines four scenarios of agreement/disagreement. The positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn are consistent across these scenarios. Focus on the third scenario (master confirms, slave denies) and the fourth (slave confirms, master denies) to see how each position handles the interplay of 'iddah trustworthiness and ownership.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical outcome: According to Abu Hanifa, if the master confirms but the slave denies, the raj'ah is not valid because the slave woman's denial prevails (the raj'ah would have to be proven by evidence). According to the Sahibayn, the master's confirmation makes the raj'ah valid. This affects the marital status and the master's rights to her usufruct.

و اذا قال زوج الامة بعد انقضاء عدتها قد كنت راجعتها وصدقه المولى وكذبه الامة فالقول قولها عند ابي حنيفة وقالا القول قول المولى لان بضعها مملوك له فقد اقربما هو خالص حقه للزوج فشابه الاقرار عليها بالنكاح وهو يقول حكم الرجعة يبتنى على العدة والقول فى العدة قولها فكذافيما يبتنى عليها ولو كان على القلب فعندهما القول قول المولى وكذا عنده في الصحيح لانها منقضية العدة في الحال وقد ظهر ملک المتعة للمولى ولا يقبل قولها فى ابطاله بخلاف الوجه الاول لان المولى بالتصديق في الرجعة مقر بقيام العدة عندها ولا يظهر ملكه مع العدة وان قالت قد انقضت عدتى وقال الزوج والمولى لم تنقض عدتک فالقول قولها لانها امينة فى ذلك اذهى العالمة به

صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ عدت گزر جانے کے بعد منافع بضع مولی کے مملوک ہیں۔ پس شوہر کے لئے منافع بضع کا اقرار خالص اپنے حق کا اقرار کرنا ہے۔ لہذا اس کو رد نہیں کیا جا سکتا اور یہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسا کہ مولیٰ نے اپنی باندی پر نکاح کا اقرار کیا ہو۔ مثلاً کہا کہ میں نے اپنی باندی کا فلاں سے نکاح کر دیا تو اس اقرار میں مولی کا قول معتبر ہو گا۔ پس ایسے ہی یہاں بھی مولیٰ کا قول معتبر ہو گا۔ امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ رجعت کا حکم بقاء عدت اور انقضاء عدت پر مبنی ہے۔ (یعنی اگر عدت باقی ہے تو رجعت ثابت ہو جائے گی اور اگر عدت گزرگئی تو حکم رجعت ثابت نہیں ہو گا ) اور عدت کی بقاء اور عدم بقاء میں عورت کا قول معتبر ہے۔ پس جو چیز عدت پر مبنی ہوگی یعنی رجعت اس میں بھی عورت ہی کا قول معتبر ہوگا۔

Until she does not give news of the completion of the waiting period, so it is understood that the statement of revocable return is earlier and giving news of the completion of the waiting period is later, and revocable return during the waiting period is valid. For this reason we said that the revocable return is valid, and since the statement of revocable return is before the news of the end of the waiting period, for this reason if the husband said to his wife, 'I have divorced you,' and the woman replied, 'My waiting period has already passed,' then in this case divorce occurs, because the statement of divorce precedes the news of the end of the waiting period. And Imam Abu Hanifa's evidence is that the statement of revocable return is found after the waiting period has passed. What is in her womb, the one who informs about it is trustworthy. Accordingly, the divine command is: 'And it is not lawful for them to conceal what Allah has created in their wombs.' In any case, when the woman gives news of the completion of the waiting period, this news indicates that the completion of the waiting period precedes the news. Now as for how much it precedes, the answer is that among the conditions of the end of the waiting period, the closest is the husband's statement. Therefore, the woman's news will take precedence over the husband's statement 'I have taken you back.' So the conclusion is that the waiting period passed first and the revocable return occurred later, and a revocable return after the waiting period is not valid. Therefore, Imam Sahib says that this revocable return is not valid. And it is the answer to the Qiyas of the Sahibayn from the issue of divorce. The essence of the answer is that the issue of divorce is also disputed like the issue of revocable return. So how can it be correct to analogize from it? And if it is conceded that the issue of divorce is agreed upon, the answer is that after the waiting period, divorce occurs by the husband's acknowledgment, but after the waiting period, revocable return is not established by the husband's acknowledgment because there is suspicion due to interference in the right of another. So it is understood that there is a difference between these two issues and analogizing with a difference is not correct. Therefore, this analogy will not be correct. And when the husband of a slave woman said after her waiting period had passed, 'I had taken her back,' and the master confirmed him, and the slave woman denied, whose statement is accepted? The statements of jurists. And when the husband of the slave woman said after her waiting period had passed, 'I had taken her back,' and the master confirmed him, and the slave woman denied him, then according to Abu Hanifa her statement is accepted, and the two said that the master's statement is accepted because her sexual enjoyment is owned by him. So he acknowledged what is his exclusive right to the husband, so it resembles his acknowledgment of marriage upon her. And he says the ruling of revocable return is based on the waiting period, and the statement regarding the waiting period is hers, so likewise in what is based on it. And if it were the opposite, according to the two the statement of the master is accepted, and likewise according to him in the sound narration because she is immediately one whose waiting period has expired and the ownership of usufruct has become apparent for the master, and her statement is not accepted in nullifying it, unlike the first face because the master by confirming the revocable return acknowledges the existence of the waiting period with her, and his ownership does not appear with the waiting period. And if she said, 'My waiting period has expired,' and the husband and master said, 'Your waiting period has not expired,' then her statement is accepted because she is trustworthy in that, as she is the one who knows about it. Translation: And when the husband of a slave woman said after her waiting period had passed, 'I had taken her back (during the waiting period),' and the master confirmed him and the slave woman denied him, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa the statement of the slave woman (is authoritative) and the Sahibayn said that the master's statement (is accepted). Because the slave woman's sexual enjoyment is owned by the master. So the master acknowledged for the husband something that is exclusively owned by the master. So it resembles (the master's) acknowledging marriage upon his slave woman. And Imam Sahib says that the ruling of revocable return is based on the waiting period, and during the waiting period the woman's statement (is accepted). So likewise in this matter which is based on the waiting period. And if the case is reversed, according to the Sahibayn the master's statement (is authoritative) and likewise according to Imam Sahib in the sound narration because the slave woman is currently one who has completed her waiting period, and the ownership of usufruct has become apparent for the master. So the slave woman's statement will not be accepted in nullifying the master's (right), unlike the first face because the master, by confirming the revocable return, is acknowledging the existence of the waiting period at that time, and the master's ownership does not appear with the waiting period (so the master's statement will not be authoritative). And if the slave woman said, 'My waiting period has passed,' and the husband and master said, 'Your waiting period has not passed,' then the slave woman's statement (is authoritative) because the slave woman is trustworthy in this statement (I have completed my waiting period) because the slave woman is the one who knows the end of the waiting period. Explanation: If the husband of a slave woman said after her waiting period had passed, 'I have taken you back during the waiting period,' then there are four scenarios: (1) Both the master and the slave woman confirm him, or both deny him. (4) Or the master confirms and the slave woman denies, or the opposite, i.e., the master denies and the slave woman confirms. In the first scenario, the revocable return is valid by consensus, and in the second scenario, the revocable return is not valid by consensus unless the husband brings evidence. And in the third scenario, if the husband does not have evidence, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa the statement of the slave woman is authoritative, and Imam Zufar, Imam Shafi'i, Imam Malik, and Imam Ahmad are of the same view, while according to the Sahibayn the master's statement is authoritative. The evidence of the Sahibayn is that after the waiting period has passed, the usufruct of the sexual enjoyment belongs to the master. So the husband's acknowledgment of the usufruct of the sexual enjoyment is acknowledging his own exclusive right, so it cannot be rejected. And it becomes like a case where the master has acknowledged marriage upon his slave woman. For example, he said, 'I have married my slave woman to so-and-so,' then in that acknowledgment the master's statement is authoritative. So likewise here the master's statement is authoritative. Imam Abu Hanifa's evidence is that the ruling of revocable return is based on the waiting period and the waiting period having passed. (That is, if the waiting period remains, revocable return is proven, and if the waiting period has passed, the ruling of revocable return is not proven.) And regarding the existence or non-existence of the waiting period, the woman's statement is authoritative. So whatever is based on the waiting period, i.e., revocable return, in that also the woman's statement is authoritative. And the fourth scenario, which the author of Al-Hidayah has expressed as 'and if it were the opposite,' i.e., the slave woman confirmed the husband and the master denied, then according to the Sahibayn the master's statement is authoritative. The evidence is that the usufruct of the sexual enjoyment is the exclusive right of the master, and the husband

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah discusses the disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), regarding the validity of a husband's claim of revocable return (رجعت) after the wife's 'iddah has allegedly expired, specifically in the context of a slave woman (امہ) with her master (مولیٰ). The passage first establishes a general principle: a woman's statement about her own 'iddah is accepted because she is the one who knows it, supported by the Quranic verse 'And it is not lawful for them to conceal what Allah has created in their wombs.' This principle is applied to a free woman, and then extended to a slave woman. The core issue is: when the husband of a slave woman claims he took her back during her 'iddah, but the master confirms him while the slave woman herself denies it, whose statement is accepted? Abu Hanifa says the slave woman's statement is authoritative because the ruling of revocable return depends on the 'iddah, and the woman is the authority on her 'iddah. The Sahibayn argue that the master's statement takes precedence because after the 'iddah, the usufruct of the slave woman's body (بضع) belongs to the master, and the master's acknowledgment of the revocable return is akin to his acknowledgment of a marriage contract over his slave woman—both are acknowledgments of his exclusive property rights. The passage then enumerates four possible scenarios of agreement or disagreement among the husband, master, and slave woman, explaining each ruling. In the scenario where the master confirms and the slave denies (the one just discussed), Abu Hanifa and several other Imams hold that the slave woman's statement is authoritative, while the Sahibayn hold that the master's is. The Sahibayn's evidence is based on ownership of usufruct. Abu Hanifa's evidence is based on the woman's trustworthiness regarding her 'iddah. The passage also discusses the reverse scenario where the slave woman confirms the husband and the master denies, concluding that the master's statement is authoritative according to the Sahibayn because the usufruct is his exclusive right. The overall reasoning shows how the Hanafi school balances the woman's inherent knowledge of her own 'iddah against the master's ownership rights.

Key Terms
رجعت (raj'ah): Revocable return of a wife to her husband during the 'iddah period after a revocable divorce.
عدت ('iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarrying.
مولیٰ (mawla): The master or owner of a slave woman.
بضع (bud'): Sexual enjoyment or the right to sexual intercourse within marriage.
منافع بضع (manafi' al-bud'): The usufruct or benefits derived from a slave woman's sexual capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for Imam Abu Hanifa's opinion that the slave woman's statement is accepted when she denies the husband's claim of revocable return after her 'iddah?
A) The master's ownership of the usufruct
B) The husband's right to revoke the divorce
C) The woman's trustworthiness regarding her own 'iddah, supported by Quran 2:228
D) The principle that the defendant's statement is accepted

Key Takeaway
In disputes over revocable return of a slave woman after 'iddah, Imam Abu Hanifa accepts the slave woman's statement, while the Sahibayn accept the master's statement, based on the woman's trustworthiness about her 'iddah vs. the master's ownership of usufruct.

Disputed Raj'a After Slave Woman's 'Idda: Four Scenarios
Explains four scenarios of disputed raj'a with master and slave woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the four scenarios of disputed raj'a after a slave woman's 'idda and state the Hanafi position (Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn) in each scenario.

Core Idea
Testimony in raj'a disputes balances the master's ownership with the woman's authority over her own 'idda.

This passage from the chapter on Talaq of the Sick presents a nuanced case: when a husband claims he performed raj'a (took back his slave wife) during her 'idda, but the 'idda has now expired. The master and the slave woman may disagree with each other, and the question is whose word is accepted. The passage outlines four possible scenarios and the differing views of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn. Understanding these distinctions illustrates the Hanafi method of balancing ownership rights with procedural deference to the woman's knowledge of her own 'idda.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios
Read the passage and list the four combinations of testimony from the master and the slave woman: both confirm, both deny, master confirms and slave denies, master denies and slave confirms.
The first two scenarios are by consensus; the dispute is in the latter two.
2
State the Hanafi position in each disputed scenario
For the third scenario (master confirms, slave denies), note that Imam Abu Hanifa accepts the slave woman's word, while the Sahibayn accept the master's. For the fourth scenario (master denies, slave confirms), both Imam Abu Hanifa (correct narration) and the Sahibayn accept the master's word.
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that raj'a depends on 'idda, and the woman's word is accepted regarding 'idda.
3
Analyze the evidence for each view
Study the Sahibayn's evidence: after 'idda, the benefits of the pudendum are the master's property, so his affirmation of his own right cannot be rejected. Then study Imam Abu Hanifa's evidence: raj'a is based on 'idda, and the woman's word is accepted regarding 'idda. In the fourth scenario, accepting the slave woman's word would void the master's ownership without proof.
Notice the shift in reasoning between scenarios based on whether the master admits the existence of 'idda.
4
Compare with other schools
Note that Imam Zufar, Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad agree with Imam Abu Hanifa on the third scenario. This shows the issue is not uniquely Hanafi.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in a court case, if the master confirms the husband's claim, the slave woman's denial will be accepted (per Abu Hanifa), meaning raj'a is not established. If the master denies, his word is accepted (both views), so raj'a is also not established. The husband loses both ways unless he brings evidence.
This shows the importance of the master's stance in determining the outcome.

Source Text
