Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Ruju' After Incomplete Ghusl
Explains intra-Hanafi disagreement on ruju' after ghusl with unwashed limb parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on ruju' after incomplete ghusl, distinguish between a complete limb (عضو کامل) and less than a limb (ما دون العضو), and apply the istihsan-based ruling to determine whether the husband's right of return is preserved.

Core Idea
The size of the unwashed body part determines whether the husband's ruju' remains: complete limb preserves ruju'; less than a limb cuts it off by precaution.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad regarding the husband's right to return to his wife (ruju') when she completes her iddah but leaves a part of her body unwashed during ghusl. The disagreement hinges on whether the unwashed part is a complete limb (عضو کامل) or less than a limb (ما دون العضو), and how qiyas and istihsan interact to produce the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the disputed ruling
The woman has completed her third menstruation and takes ghusl but leaves a part unwashed. Does the husband still have the right to return (ruju')? Note that the iddah is less than ten days.
The ruling affects the validity of the husband's return or the woman's remarriage.
2
Understand the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Abu Yusuf applies qiyas for a complete limb (ruju' cut off) then istihsan to preserve it. Muhammad applies qiyas for less than a limb (ruju' remains) then istihsan to cut it off.
Both imams ultimately agree on the outcome but differ in their starting point of analogy.
3
Analyze the rationale: qiyas vs istihsan, and the limb size distinction
The key factor is whether the unwashed part dries quickly and is habitually overlooked. Less than a limb dries quickly, so precaution (istihsan) treats it as washed. A complete limb dries slowly and is habitually attended to, so it is considered unwashed.
This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction between the two imams.
4
Apply the ruling to specific cases: rinsing mouth and nose
Two narrations from Abu Yusuf: one treats rinsing mouth/nose as a complete limb (ruju' remains); the other (preferred) treats them as less than a limb (ruju' cut off), aligning with Muhammad's view.
The preferred view considers these acts as less than a limb due to their small size and quick drying.

اور امام محمد کے نزدیک محل قیاس ما دون العضو ہے۔ چنانچہ امام محمد فرماتے ہیں کہ مادون العضو میں قیاس کا تقاضا یہ ہے کہ رجعت باقی رہے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ مادون العضو خشک رہنے کی صورت میں بھی حدث باقی ہے۔ اس لئے کہ جنابت اور حیض کا حکم متجری اور ٹکڑے ٹکڑے نہیں ہوتا پس جب ایک جز میں حدث باقی ہے اگر چہ مادون العضو ہے تو ہم کہیں گے کہ پورے بدن میں حدث ہے اور جب پورے بدن میں حدث ( حیض) باقی ہے تو عدت باقی ہے اور جب عدت باقی ہے تو رجعت کا حکم بھی باقی ہے۔ اور وجہ استحسان اور یہی عضو کامل اور مادون العضو میں وجہ فرق ہے کہ عضو سے کم قلت کی وجہ سے بہت جلد خشک ہو جاتا ہے تو اس حصہ تک پانی نہ پہنچنے کا یقین نہیں ہوسکتا کیونکہ ہو سکتا ہے کہ اس حصہ کو دھویا ہومگر جلد ہی خشک ہو گیا اس لئے ہم نے احتیاط پر عمل کرتے ہوئے کہا کہ رجعت کا حکم منقطع ہو گیا اور احتیاط ہی کے پیش نظر یہ حکم ہے کہ یہ عورت ایسی حالت میں دوسرے شوہر سے نکاح نہ کرے اگر کر لیا تو حلال نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ ممکن ہے کہ اس حصہ تک حقیقتا پانی نہ پہنچا ہو اور حدث باقی رہنے کی وجہ سے عدت باقی ہو تو ایسی صورت میں یہ نکاح معتدة الغیر کے ساتھ ہوگا اور یہ نا جائز ہے۔
اس کے برخلاف اگر عضو کامل خشک رہا تو رجعت کا حکم منقطع نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ عضو کامل بہت جلد خشک نہیں ہوتا اور عادتا عضو کامل سے انسان غافل بھی نہیں رہتا۔ لہذا یہی کہا جائے گا کہ ابھی تک اس حصہ کو دھویا نہیں گیا اور جب ایسا ہے تو غسل نا مکمل ہونے کی وجہ سے عدت باقی ہے اور عدت میں رجعت کا حکم باقی رہتا ہے۔ اس وجہ سے اس صورت میں حکم رجعت منقطع نہیں ہو گا ۔ پس عضو کامل اور مادون العضو میں فرق واضح ہو گیا۔ اور اگر دس روز سے کم میں کوئی عورت تیسرے حیض سے فارغ ہوئی اور غسل کر لیا لیکن کلی کرنا یا ناک میں پانی ڈالنا چھوڑ دیا تو اس میں امام ابو یوسف سے دو روایتیں ہیں۔ ایک روایت میں ہے کہ رجعت منقطع نہیں ہوگی۔ جیسا کہ عضو کامل کو چھوڑ نے سے رجعت منقطع نہیں ہوتی۔ اس روایت کے مطابق کلی کرنا اور ناک میں پانی ڈالنا ان میں سے ہر ایک عضو کامل کے مرتبہ میں ہے۔ امام ابو یوسف سے اس کو ہشام نے روایت کیا ہے اور امام ابو یوسف سے دوسری روایت جس کو امام کرخی نے روایت کیا یہ ہے کہ رجعت منقطع ہو جائے گی اور کلی کرنے یا ناک میں پانی ڈالنے کو چھوڑنا مادون العضو کو چھوڑنے کے مانند ہے۔ کیونکہ غسل میں کلی کرنے اور ناک میں پانی ڈالنے کی فرضیت میں اختلاف ہے۔ چنانچہ حضرت امام مالک اور امام شافعی کے نزدیک کلی کرنا اور ناک میں پانی ڈالنا غسل اور وضو دونوں میں سنت ہے اور ہمارے نزدیک غسل میں فرض اور وضو میں سنت ہے۔ اس کے برخلاف دوسرے اعضاء کہ ان کی فرضیت میں کسی کا اختلاف نہیں۔ یہ دوسری روایت امام محمد کا بھی قول ہے۔
Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary of Hidayah Volume 5. It has been narrated that according to Abu Yusuf, the locus of analogy is the limb and above the limb. Thus, Imam Abu Yusuf says: In the case of a complete limb, the requirement of analogy is that the right of return does not remain. The proof is that the woman has washed most of her body, and the rule is 'the majority takes the ruling of the whole', i.e., the majority is treated as the whole. Therefore, it is as if water reached the entire body, and when water reached the entire body, the waiting period has passed, and after the passing of the waiting period, the ruling of return does not remain. So the requirement of analogy is that in this case, the return does not remain. And istihsan (juristic preference) is that if the limb or above the limb remains dry, the return is not cut off, because due to the lack of purification, the waiting period remains, and during the waiting period, the ruling of return remains. And according to Imam Muhammad, the locus of analogy is less than the limb. Thus, Imam Muhammad says: In the case of less than a limb, the requirement of analogy is that the return remains. The proof is that even when less than a limb remains dry, the state of ritual impurity remains, because the ruling of major impurity and menstruation is not divisible into parts. So when impurity remains in one part, even if it is less than a limb, we will say that the entire body is impure, and when the entire body remains impure (menstruation), the waiting period remains, and when the waiting period remains, the ruling of return remains. And the reason for istihsan and the basis of difference between a complete limb and less than a limb is that less than a limb dries very quickly due to its small size, so it cannot be certain that water did not reach that part, because it is possible that the part was washed but quickly dried. Therefore, we acted on precaution and said that the ruling of return is cut off. And due to this precaution, the ruling is that this woman should not marry another man in this state; if she does marry, it will not be permissible, because it is possible that water actually did not reach that part, and due to the continued impurity, the waiting period remains, so in such a case, this marriage would be with a woman in her waiting period, and it is impermissible. In contrast, if a complete limb remains dry, the ruling of return is not cut off, because a complete limb does not dry quickly, and habitually a person is not neglectful of a complete limb. Therefore, it will be said that this part has not yet been washed, and when that is the case, due to incomplete bathing, the waiting period remains, and during the waiting period, the ruling of return remains. For this reason, in this case, the ruling of return is not cut off. Thus, the difference between a complete limb and less than a limb is clear. And if a woman finishes her third menstruation in less than ten days and takes a bath but omits rinsing the mouth or inserting water into the nose, then regarding this, there are two narrations from Imam Abu Yusuf. In one narration, it is said that the return will not be cut off, just as omitting a complete limb does not cut off the return. According to this narration, rinsing the mouth and inserting water into the nose are each at the level of a complete limb. This narration was transmitted by Hisham from Imam Abu Yusuf. And the second narration from Imam Abu Yusuf, transmitted by Imam al-Karkhi, is that the return will be cut off, and omitting rinsing the mouth or inserting water into the nose is like omitting less than a limb, because there is a difference of opinion regarding the obligatory nature of rinsing the mouth and inserting water into the nose in bathing. Thus, according to Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i, rinsing the mouth and inserting water into the nose are Sunnah in both bathing and ablution, whereas according to us, they are obligatory in bathing and Sunnah in ablution. This is contrary to other limbs, regarding the obligation of which there is no disagreement. This second narration is also the saying of Imam Muhammad.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad regarding whether a husband's right to return to his wife (ruju') is cut off if she, after completing her third menstruation within less than ten days, takes a bath but leaves a certain part of her body unwashed. The dispute revolves around the size of the unwashed part. Abu Yusuf applies qiyas (analogy) to the case where most of the body is washed (which ends the waiting period) and extends that to a complete limb being left dry, but then uses istihsan (juristic preference) to rule that if a complete limb or more is left dry, ruju' is not cut off because impurity remains. Imam Muhammad, on the other hand, uses qiyas for less than a limb, concluding that ruju' remains because the impurity of menstruation is indivisible. The passage further clarifies the difference: less than a limb dries quickly, so one cannot be certain it wasn't washed, hence precaution demands treating it as washed and cutting off ruju'. For a complete limb, it is unlikely to be overlooked and dries slowly, so it is considered unwashed, and ruju' remains. The passage ends with two narrations from Abu Yusuf about rinsing the mouth and nose, which are considered by some as having the status of a complete limb or less than a limb, affecting the ruling. The relied-upon position is not explicitly stated here, but the second narration aligns with Imam Muhammad's view.

Key Terms
عضو کامل (‘udw kāmil): A complete limb, e.g., hand, foot, etc., which is large and dries slowly, not easily overlooked.
ما دون العضو (mā dūn al-‘udw): Less than a limb, a small body part like a finger or a spot, which dries quickly and may be washed unintentionally.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference, overriding the strict analogy due to practical considerations or precaution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman completes her third menstruation, performs ghusl, but leaves a small patch of skin (less than a limb) dry. According to the preferred Hanafi view, the husband's right to return (ruju') is:
A) Preserved because the impurity is indivisible.
B) Cut off due to precaution (istihsan).
C) Preserved because the ghusl is valid.
D) Cut off because the ghusl is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The husband's right to return after incomplete ghusl depends on whether the unwashed part is a complete limb (ruju' remains) or less (ruju' cut off), due to istihsan.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Right of Ruju' When Husband Denies Intercourse with Pregnant Wife
Ruling with proof: husband's denial of intercourse does not affect right of ruju'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a husband's right of ruju' when he denies intercourse with his pregnant wife, and explain the proof from the hadith 'al-walad li al-firash'.

Core Idea
Denial of intercourse does not negate the right to return — the child's existence legally proves consummation.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a man divorces his wife while she is pregnant or after she has given birth from him, and then claims he never had intercourse with her. Despite his denial, the Hanafi school rules that he still has the right to take her back (ruju') during the waiting period. The proof lies in the hadith 'al-walad li al-firash', which attributes the child to the husband and thus legally establishes that intercourse occurred.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
State the act in question: a husband divorces his pregnant wife and denies intercourse. The ruling: he has the right of ruju' (to take her back).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith 'al-walad li al-firash' (the child belongs to the bed). Identify this as the source of evidence.
The hadith is a hadith ahad, but it is widely accepted.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the hadith proves intercourse: the child is attributed to the husband (the owner of the bed), which necessitates that intercourse occurred, since a child cannot exist without it.
This is a legal presumption that overrides the husband's verbal denial.
4
Apply the consequence
Once intercourse is established, the marriage is considered consummated (milk muakkad). In a revocable divorce, the husband has the right to return to his wife during the iddah. Therefore, ruju' is valid.
The husband's claim is nullified by Shari'ah's presumption.
5
Note the condition
The rule applies only if the wife gave birth before the divorce. If she gives birth after, the iddah ends with birth and ruju' is no longer possible.
This is the interpretation (ta'wil) given in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب طلاق المريض
و من طلق امرأته وهي حامل اوولدت منه وقال لم اجامعها فله الرجعة لان الحبل متى ظهر في مدة يتصوران يكون منه جعل منه لقوله عليه السلام الولد للفراش وذالك دليل الوطى منه كذا اذا ثبت نسب الولد منه جعل واطياو اذابت الوطى تاكد الملک و الطلاق فى ملك متاكد يعقب الرجعة ويبطل زعمه بتكذيب الشرع الايرى انه يثبت بهذا الوطى الاحصان فلان تثبت به الرجعة اولى وتاويل مسألة الولادة ان تلد قبل الطلاق لانه لو ولدت بعده تنقضى العدة بالولادة فلاتتصور الرجعة
ترجمہ: اور جس شخص نے اپنی بیوی کو طلاق دی حالانکہ وہ حاملہ ہے یا اس نے اس سے بچہ جنا اور اس شخص نے کہا کہ میں نے اس سے جماع نہیں کیا ہے تو اس شخص کو رجعت کا اختیار ہے۔ کیونکہ حمل جب اتنی مدت میں ظاہر ہوا کہ شوہر سے ہونا ممکن ہے تو وہ شوہر ہی کا قرار دیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کا ارشاد ہے کہ بچہ تو فراش کا ہے اور یہ اس مرد سے وطی کی دلیل ہے اور ایسے ہی جب بچہ کا نسب اس شوہر سے ثابت ہوا تو اس کو وطی کرنے والا قرار دیا جائے گا اور جب وطی ثابت ہوئی تو ملک موکد ہو گئی اور ملک موکد میں طلاق کے بعد رجعت ہوتی ہے اور اس کا قول ( کہ میں نے جماع نہیں کیا) شریعت کے جھٹلانے سے باطل ہو جائے گا۔ کیا نہیں دیکھتا کہ اس وطی سے احصان ثابت ہو جاتا ہے تو اس سے رجعت بدرجہ اولیٰ ثابت ہو جائے گی اور ولادت کے مسئلہ کی تاویل یہ ہے کہ طلاق دینے سے پہلے اس نے بچہ جنا۔ کیونکہ اگر طلاق کے بعد جنا تو ولادت سے عدت گزرگئی پھر رجعت ممکن نہ رہی۔
تشریح: صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص نے اپنی بیوی کو طلاق دی حالانکہ اس کی یہ بیوی حاملہ ہے یا اس نے قبل الطلاق نکاح میں رہتے ہوئے بچہ جنا اور یہ شخص کہتا ہے کہ میں نے اپنی اس بیوی کے ساتھ جماع نہیں کیا ہے اس کے باوجود اگر یہ شخص رجعت کرنا چاہے تو شریعت کی جانب سے اس کو رجعت کا پورا پورا اختیار ہے اور اس کا قول کہ میں نے جماع نہیں کیا شرعا غیر معتبر ہے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ حمل جب اتنی مدت میں ظاہر ہو گیا کہ اس کو شوہر کا قرار دینا ممکن ہے تو اس حمل کو شوہر کا قرار دے دیا جائے گا۔ مثلاً طلاق دینے کے بعد چھ ماہ سے کم میں بچہ پیدا ہو گیا تو اس بچہ کا نسب اسی طلاق دینے والے شخص سے ثابت ہوگا۔ کیونکہ ثابت ہو گیا کہ یہ عورت طلاق کے دن حاملہ تھی اور اس بچہ کا نسب اس شخص سے اس لئے ثابت ہوگا کہ حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے فرمایا ہے: الـــولـــد للفراش یعنی بچہ تو فراش کا ہے اور ایک حدیث میں ہے الولد للفراش وللعامر الحجر یعنی بچہ تو فراش کا ہے اور زنا کار کے لئے پتھر ہے۔
Chapter of Divorce of the Sick. And whoever divorces his wife while she is pregnant or she gives birth from him and he says, 'I did not have intercourse with her,' then he has the right of return (ruju'), because if the pregnancy appears within a period in which it is conceivable to be from him, it is attributed to him due to his saying, peace be upon him, 'The child belongs to the bed.' That is evidence of intercourse from him. Similarly, when the lineage of the child is established from him, he is considered as one who had intercourse. And when intercourse is established, the ownership (marital bond) becomes confirmed, and divorce in a confirmed ownership is followed by the right of return. And his claim (that he did not have intercourse) is nullified by the contradiction of the Shari'ah. Do you not see that by this intercourse, ihsan (protection against hadd punishment) is established, so it is more appropriate that the right of return is established. And the interpretation of the issue of birth is that she gave birth before the divorce, because if she gave birth after it, the waiting period would end with the birth, and then the right of return is not conceivable. Translation: And whoever divorces his wife while she is pregnant or she gives birth from him and he says, 'I did not have intercourse with her,' then he has the option of return. Because when the pregnancy appears within such a period that it is possible to be from the husband, it will be attributed to the husband, because the Holy Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'The child belongs to the bed,' and this is evidence of intercourse from this man. Similarly, when the lineage of the child is established from this husband, he will be considered as one who had intercourse. And when intercourse is established, marital ownership becomes confirmed, and after divorce in confirmed ownership, return (ruju') occurs. And his statement (that he did not have intercourse) will be rendered void by the rejection of the Shari'ah. Do you not see that by this intercourse, ihsan is established, so it is more appropriate that the right of return is established. And the interpretation of the issue of birth is that she gave birth before the divorce, because if she gave birth after it, the waiting period would end with the birth, and then the right of return would not be possible. Elaboration: The scenario is that a man divorces his wife while she is pregnant or she gave birth before the divorce while in wedlock, and this man says, 'I did not have intercourse with my wife.' Despite this, if this man wants to take her back, the Shari'ah gives him full authority to take her back, and his statement that he did not have intercourse is legally unacceptable. The proof is that when the pregnancy appears within such a period that it is possible to attribute it to the husband, then that pregnancy will be attributed to the husband. For example, if after divorce a child is born in less than six months, the lineage of that child is established from the divorcing man, because it is established that this woman was pregnant on the day of divorce. And the lineage of that child is established from this man because the Holy Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'The child belongs to the bed' — meaning the child belongs to the bed; and in another hadith: 'The child belongs to the bed, and for the fornicator is the stone.'
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with its proof. The scenario is a man who divorces his wife who is either pregnant at the time of divorce or gave birth to a child from him before the divorce. The husband claims he never had intercourse with her. Despite his denial, Shari'ah grants him the right to take her back (ruju'). The proof is based on the hadith 'al-walad li al-firash' (the child belongs to the bed), which establishes that the child's lineage is attributed to the husband, and this attribution legally proves that intercourse occurred. Once intercourse is established, the marriage is considered consummated (milk muakkad), and in a consummated marriage, after divorce, the husband has the right to take his wife back during the waiting period. The husband's denial is overridden by the legal presumption established by the existence of the child and the hadith. The text also clarifies that if the wife gave birth after the divorce, the waiting period ends with the birth, and ruju' is no longer possible. Therefore, the issue applies only when the birth occurs before the divorce. This ruling demonstrates the strength of the legal maxim 'al-walad li al-firash' and its effect on other rulings.

Key Terms
رجعة (ruj‘a (or raj‘a)): The husband's right to take back his wife during the waiting period after a revocable divorce.
فراش (firāsh): The marital bed, metaphor for the husband; the hadith means the child is attributed to the husband.
ملک موکد (milk mu’akkad): Confirmed marital ownership, meaning a consummated marriage where full rights and duties apply.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man divorces his pregnant wife and claims he never had intercourse with her. According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on his right of ruju'?
A) He has no right of ruju' because he denies intercourse.
B) He has the right of ruju' because the child establishes that intercourse occurred.
C) He has the right of ruju' only if the wife confirms intercourse.
D) He has no right of ruju' until the child is born and lineage is confirmed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the existence of a child from the marital bed legally proves intercourse, giving the husband the right to take back his wife during the iddah even if he denies intercourse.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

Ruling on Divorcing a Pregnant Wife with Denial of Intercourse
Ruling with proof on return after divorce of pregnant wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of return (raj'ah) when a husband divorces his pregnant wife and denies intercourse, and explain the proof from the hadith 'al-walad lil-firash'.

Core Idea
Denial of intercourse does not negate the right of return; the shariah's evidence from the bed overrides the husband's claim.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in family law: a husband divorces his pregnant wife and then claims he never had intercourse with her. The Hanafi school addresses this by prioritizing the legal evidence from the marital bed over the husband's denial. Through this passage, you will learn how the prophetic hadith 'al-walad lil-firash' establishes a confirmed marital bond, thereby preserving the husband's right to take back his wife during the waiting period.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is a husband divorcing his pregnant wife while denying intercourse. The ruling is that he still has the right of return (raj'ah).
The key is that the husband's denial does not affect the ruling.
2
Locate the evidence
The primary proof is the prophetic hadith: 'al-walad lil-firash' (the child belongs to the bed). This hadith is cited in the passage.
This is a hadith ahad, but it is accepted in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith attributes the child to the husband as the owner of the bed. This attribution implies that intercourse occurred, which in turn establishes a confirmed marital bond (milk muta'akkid). Divorce in a confirmed bond entitles the husband to return.
The inference is from the shariah's indication, not from direct testimony.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some might argue that the husband's explicit denial should override the implied evidence. The Hanafi response is that the evidence of the shariah (the hadith) is stronger than an individual's statement.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; the Hanafi position is mufta bihi.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is relied upon because the shariah's indication is definitive in establishing the legal consequence. The practical effect is that the husband cannot avoid the right of return by denying intercourse.
The passage also uses a fortiori reasoning: if this establishes ihsan (which has serious penal effects), then establishing return is even more appropriate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و من طلق امرأته وهي حامل اوولدت منه وقال لم اجامعها فله الرجعة لان الحبل متى ظهر في مدة يتصوران يكون منه جعل منه لقوله عليه السلام الولد للفراش وذالك دليل الوطى منه كذا اذا ثبت نسب الولد منه جعل واطياو اذابت الوطى تاكد الملک و الطلاق فى ملك متاكد يعقب الرجعة ويبطل زعمه بتكذيب الشرع الايرى انه يثبت بهذا الوطى الاحصان فلان تثبت به الرجعة اولى وتاويل مسألة الولادة ان تلد قبل الطلاق لانه لو ولدت بعده تنقضى العدة بالولادة فلاتتصور الرجعة
And whoever divorces his wife while she is pregnant or she gave birth to a child from him, and he said, 'I did not have intercourse with her,' then he has the right of return. Because the pregnancy, when it appears within a period in which it is conceivable that it is from him, is attributed to him due to his saying, peace be upon him, 'The child belongs to the bed,' and that is evidence of intercourse from him. Likewise, when the lineage of the child is established from him, he is considered as having had intercourse. And when intercourse is established, the ownership (of marriage) becomes confirmed, and divorce in a confirmed ownership results in the right of return. And his claim is nullified by the refutation of the shariah. Do you not see that by this intercourse, ihsan (protection of marriage) is established? Then it is more fitting that return be established by it. And the interpretation of the case of birth is that she gave birth before the divorce, because if she gave birth after it, the waiting period ends with the birth, so return is not conceivable.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding a husband who divorces his pregnant wife or a wife who has given birth to his child, and then claims he never had intercourse with her. The text establishes that despite his denial, he has the right to take her back (رجعة) during her waiting period. The proof is based on the prophetic hadith: 'The child belongs to the bed.' Since the pregnancy or birth is attributable to him by shariah (i.e., within a timeframe where it could be his), the husband is legally considered to have had intercourse, even if he denies it. This establishes a confirmed marital bond (ملك متأكد), and divorce in such a bond allows for return. The husband's claim is rendered invalid because shariah contradicts it. The text further argues a fortiori: if this presumed intercourse establishes the legal consequence of ihsan (which has penal implications), then establishing the lesser consequence of return is even more appropriate. The interpretation of the birth case is specified: she must have given birth before the divorce; if she gives birth after divorce, the waiting period ends immediately with birth, making return impossible.

Key Terms
رجعة (raj'ah): The right of the husband to take back his wife during her waiting period after a revocable divorce without a new marriage contract.
فراش (firash): The marital bed; legally, it refers to the husband's right to attribute children born during the marriage to him.
ملك متأكد (milk muta'akkid): A confirmed marital bond, established when the husband has had actual or legally presumed intercourse with his wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the husband have the right of return despite his denial of intercourse?
A) Because the wife's testimony is accepted over the husband's.
B) Because the pregnancy or birth establishes intercourse by shariah evidence, which is stronger than his denial.
C) Because the husband has admitted to the divorce, so return is automatic.
D) Because the waiting period has not yet ended.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the husband's denial of intercourse does not invalidate the right of return when the pregnancy or birth is attributable to him by shariah evidence.

Divorce after Seclusion: Husband's Denial and Subsequent Birth
Ruling on return after divorce with seclusion and denial of intercourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the right of return (رجعة) when a husband admits seclusion but denies intercourse, and explain how a later birth can overturn that denial.

Core Idea
Admission of no intercourse after seclusion blocks the right of return until disproven by birth.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in Hanafi divorce law: a husband who has valid seclusion with his wife but later denies intercourse, then divorces her. The source passage from Al-Hidayah clarifies when he retains the right of return (رجعة) and how a subsequent birth can retroactively establish intercourse, making the return valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the husband’s attempt to take back his wife (رجعة) after divorce. In the first scenario, the ruling is that he does not have the right of return.
2
Locate the dalil (reasoning)
The proof is based on the principle that confirmed ownership (ملك متأكد) requires actual intercourse. The husband admitted its absence, so he is believed regarding his own right.
The shariah does not contradict his admission because there is no independent evidence of intercourse.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The legal link: The right of return is established only when the marriage is confirmed through intercourse. Since the husband admits no intercourse, he cannot claim the right that depends on it.
Contrast with the dower (مهر): dower is confirmed by seclusion alone, because it is based on delivering the substitute, not on possession.
4
Analyze the sub-scenario (later birth)
If after the divorce she gives birth within two years minus one day, the return becomes valid. The birth proves that intercourse occurred before the divorce, establishing paternity.
The maximum pregnancy period in the Hanafi school is two years.
5
Determine the thamarah (practical effect)
The practical difference: Without birth, the husband cannot return; with birth within two years, the return is valid and paternity is established.
This also affects the wife’s iddah and inheritance rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان خلابها و اغلق بابا اوارخى ستر او قال لم اجامعهاثم طلقها لم يملك الرجعة لان تاكد الملك بالوطی وقد اقر بعدمه فيصدق في حق نفسة والرجعة حقه ولم يصر مكذبا شرعا بخلاف المهرلان تاكد المهر المسمى يبتنى على تسليم المبدل لاعلى القبض بخلاف الفصل الاول فان راجعها معناه بعد ما خلابها وقال لم اجامعها ثم جاءت بولد لاقل من سنتين بيوم صحت تلك الرجعة لانه ثبت النسب منه اذهي لم تقربا نقضاء العدة والولد يبقي في البطن هذه المدة فانزل واطياقبل الطلاق دون ما بعده لان على . اعتبار الثاني يزول الملك بنفس الطلاق لعدم الوطى قبله فيحرم الوطى والمسلم لا يفعل الحرام
For if he had seclusion with her and closed the door or let down a curtain, or said, 'I did not have intercourse with her,' then divorced her, he does not have the right of return, because the confirmation of ownership is by intercourse, and he has admitted to its absence, so he is believed with regard to himself; and return is his right, and he has not been refuted by the shariah, unlike the dower, because the confirmation of the specified dower is based on delivering the substitute, not on taking possession, unlike the first case. Then if he took her back, meaning after seclusion and he said, 'I did not have intercourse with her,' then she brought a child in less than two years minus one day, that return is valid because lineage is established from him, as she has not admitted to the completion of the waiting period, and the child can remain in the womb for this duration. Then he is considered as having had intercourse before the divorce, not after, because on the second consideration, the ownership would cease by the divorce itself due to the lack of intercourse before it, so intercourse would become forbidden, and a Muslim does not do what is forbidden.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage presents a second scenario and its ruling. If a husband has seclusion (خلوة صحيحة) with his wife (closing the door or letting down a curtain), then states that he did not have intercourse, and then divorces her, he does NOT have the right of return (رجعة). The reason is that confirmed ownership (ملك متأكد) only occurs through actual intercourse. Since the husband himself admits that no intercourse took place, he is believed regarding his own right; the return is his right, and since he denies the basis for it, he cannot claim it. The shariah does not contradict him here because there is no independent evidence of intercourse. This is distinguished from the previous case of pregnancy/birth, where the shariah provides evidence that overrides his denial. It is also distinguished from the dower (مهر): the dower becomes confirmed upon valid seclusion, even without intercourse, because it is for the benefit of the wife, not based on the husband's claim. Then the passage discusses a sub-scenario: if, after such a divorce (where he denied intercourse), he nevertheless takes her back (رجعة), and then she gives birth within less than two years from the divorce, the return is valid. This is because the child's paternity is established from him, indicating that intercourse must have occurred before the divorce (since the child could not be born after a divorce without intercourse). The wife did not admit to the completion of her waiting period (so the child could be from the marriage). The maximum period of pregnancy is two years by Hanafi opinion. Therefore, the husband is considered to have had intercourse before the divorce, making the marriage confirmed and the return valid. The alternative (that intercourse occurred after the divorce) is rejected because it would imply intercourse during a period when it was forbidden (after divorce), and a Muslim would not do that.

Key Terms
خلوت (khalwah): Valid seclusion between husband and wife in a private place where intercourse is possible, without any impediment.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband before remarriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a husband has seclusion with his wife, denies intercourse, and then divorces her, why does he not have the right of return?
A) Because seclusion is not considered a valid basis for return.
B) Because he admitted to the absence of intercourse, which is required for confirmed ownership, and he is believed regarding his own right.
C) Because the wife must also admit to intercourse for return to be possible.
D) Because the divorce was pronounced after seclusion, which invalidates return.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the husband's admission of no intercourse after seclusion blocks his right of return, but a subsequent birth within two years overrides that admission and validates the return.

Seclusion with Claim of No Intercourse: Rajʿah Denied
Ruling on seclusion with claim of no intercourse then divorce: no rajʿah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on rajʿah when a husband claims no intercourse after seclusion and divorce, and distinguish it from the ruling on dower.

Core Idea
If husband admits no intercourse after seclusion, he loses rajʿah but still owes full mahr.

This lesson examines a nuanced Hanafi ruling: when a husband has valid seclusion (khulwa sahiha) with his wife, then claims he did not have intercourse, and subsequently divorces her, does he retain the right to revoke the divorce (rajʿah)? The answer is no. The reasoning hinges on the nature of rajʿah as the husband's exclusive right and the acceptance of his admission regarding his own right. However, the dower obligation remains intact, as it is based on seclusion, not intercourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
Locate the conditions: valid seclusion, claim of no intercourse, divorce. State the ruling: no right of rajʿah.
2
Extract the proof (dalil)
The proof is istihsan: the husband's admission is accepted regarding his own right. The text says: because marital authority is confirmed through intercourse, and he admitted its absence, so he is believed regarding his own right.
The dalil is not a scriptural text but a legal maxim.
3
Understand the reasoning (wajh al-dalalah)
Rajʿah is a sole right of the husband. His claim that intercourse did not occur negates the confirmation of marital authority through intercourse, so he cannot claim rajʿah.
Contrast with dower: dower is confirmed by seclusion, not intercourse.
4
Distinguish from the case of dower
The text explicitly contrasts: the full specified dower remains due because it is based on delivery of the object (seclusion), not on possession (intercourse). Hence, the husband's admission does not affect dower.
This resolves the potential objection: if the husband is believed, why is the full dower still due?
5
Apply the ruling and note the ikhtilaf
The practical application: a husband cannot unilaterally revoke the divorce based on his claim. Some scholars may disagree, but the Hanafi view is established in Zahir al-Riwayah.
The ruling is mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان خلا بها و أغلق بابا أو أرخى سترا أو قال لم أجامعها ثم طلقها لم يملك الرجعة لان تأكد الملك بالوطي وقد أقر بعدمه فيصدق في حق نفسه والرجعة حقه ولم يصر مكذبا شرعا بخلاف المهر فإن تأكد المهر المسمى يبتني على تسليم المبدل لا على القبض بخلاف الفصل الأول
If he has seclusion with her and closes the door or lowers a curtain or says 'I did not have intercourse with her', then divorces her, he does not have the right of revocation because the confirmation of marital authority is through intercourse and he has admitted its absence, so he is believed regarding his own right, and revocation is his right, and he is not considered a liar by Sharia, unlike the dower for the confirmation of the specified dower is based on delivery of the object not on possession, contrary to the first case.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding a husband who has valid seclusion (khulwa sahiha) with his wife, then claims he did not have intercourse, and then divorces her. The question is whether he retains the right to revoke the divorce (rajʿah). The ruling is that he does not have that right. The reasoning is that the marital authority (milk al-nikah) is fully confirmed by actual intercourse; the husband has admitted that intercourse did not occur, so his admission is accepted regarding his own right (i.e., the right of rajʿah). Since revocation is solely his right, his claim is binding on himself. The text then addresses an objection: if the husband is believed, why does Sharia obligate the full specified dower (mahr musammā)? The response clarifies that the full dower is due because of valid seclusion, not because of actual intercourse. The specified dower is confirmed by the act of handing over the object (bilateral) to the husband's authority, not by physical possession. Therefore, the obligation of full dower does not contradict the husband's claim of no intercourse. In contrast, in the previous case of pregnancy and lineage, the husband's claim was invalidated because lineage cannot be established without intercourse.

Key Terms
خلوة (khulwa): Valid seclusion between husband and wife in a private place where intercourse is possible, with no legal impediment.
رجعة (rajʿah): The husband's right to take back his wife during the 'iddah after a revocable divorce (talāq rajʿī) without a new contract.
مهر مسمى (mahr musammā): The dower that is specified in the marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because he admitted that intercourse did not occur, and rajʿah depends on intercourse.
B) Because the wife denied his claim.
C) Because the divorce is irrevocable in all cases of seclusion.
D) Because the husband is lying, so he loses the right.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, after valid seclusion and a claim of no intercourse, the husband loses the right of rajʿah but must still pay the full specified dower.

Reconciliation Valid if Birth Occurs Within Two Years
Ruling on reconciliation after seclusion and claim, then birth within two years validates the reconciliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that reconciliation is valid if the wife gives birth within two years of divorce after seclusion, and explain the istihsan reasoning based on the principle that a Muslim does not commit haram.

Core Idea
If a child is born within two years, we presume intercourse before divorce to avoid attributing haram.

This passage addresses a nuanced scenario in divorce law: after a husband secludes with his wife and later claims he did not have intercourse, he reconciles with her. Subsequently, she gives birth to a child within two years of the divorce. According to the Hanafi school, the reconciliation is valid. The reasoning relies on the established maximum pregnancy period of two years and the principle of istihsan (juristic preference) that avoids assuming a Muslim committed a forbidden act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
What is the factual situation? The husband secludes with his wife, later claims no intercourse, then reconciles (raj'ah). The wife gives birth to a child within two years (less than the Hanafi maximum pregnancy period) from the divorce. The ruling is that the reconciliation is valid.
2
Understand the legal problem
If we assumed that intercourse occurred after the divorce, it would be haram because the marriage had ended and there was no prior intercourse to establish that the 'iddah was not over. This creates a conflict with the possibility of establishing lineage.
3
Apply the principle of istihsan
To resolve the conflict, we presume that intercourse occurred before the divorce, because a Muslim does not commit haram. This presumption (istihsan) validates the reconciliation and establishes the child's lineage from the husband.
The principle is 'المسلم لا يفعل الحرام' (a Muslim does not commit the forbidden).
4
Note the significance of the two-year period
The Hanafi school holds that a child can remain in the womb for a maximum of two years. Therefore, a birth within two years of the divorce can be attributed to the husband, supporting the presumption that intercourse occurred before the divorce.
Other schools have different maximums (e.g., Shafi'i: 4 years; Maliki: 5 years; Hanbali: preferably 1 year).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان راجعها معه بعد ما خلا بها وقال لم أجامعها ثم جاءت بولد لأقل من سنتين بيوم صحت تلك الرجعة لأنه ثبت النسب منه إذ هي لم تقر بانقضاء العدة والولد يبقى في البطن هذه المدة فأنزل وطيئا قبل الطلاق دون ما بعده لأن على اعتبار الثاني يزول الملك بنفس الطلاق لعدم الوطي قبله فيحرم الوطي والمسلم لا يفعل الحرام
If he reconciles with her after he had seclusion and said 'I did not have intercourse with her', then she gives birth to a child in less than two years from the divorce, that reconciliation is valid because lineage from him is established, since she did not admit the completion of the 'iddah, and the child can remain in the womb for this period. So we consider that intercourse occurred before the divorce, not after, because on the second consideration the marital authority ceases by the divorce itself due to no intercourse before it, so intercourse becomes forbidden, and a Muslim does not commit the forbidden.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where after seclusion and a claim of no intercourse, the husband reconciles (performs rajʿah) with his wife. Subsequently, the wife gives birth to a child within two years (less than the maximum pregnancy period according to Hanafi school) from the date of divorce. The ruling is that the reconciliation is valid. The reasoning is that the child's lineage is established from this husband (since he is the only possible father). The wife did not admit that her 'iddah had ended, and the maximum pregnancy period in Hanafi fiqh is two years. Therefore, we conclude that intercourse took place before the divorce, because if we assumed it occurred after, then the marriage would have ended with the divorce (since no intercourse before it), and the post-divorce intercourse would be forbidden (haram). Since a Muslim does not commit haram, the only plausible conclusion is that the intercourse happened before the divorce, thus confirming that the marriage was still intact at that time, making the reconciliation valid. This ruling uses the principle of istihsan (juristic preference) to preserve the validity of the reconciliation and avoid attributing sin to the Muslim.

Key Terms
نسب (nasab): Lineage of the child from the father, established through valid marriage and possible intercourse.
عدة (’iddah): Waiting period after divorce or death, during which a woman cannot remarry.
الولد يبقى في البطن هذه المدة (al-walad yabqā fī al-baṭn hādhih al-muddah): The child can remain in the womb for up to two years (Hanafi view).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the wife admitted the 'iddah ended.
C) Because we presume intercourse occurred before the divorce to avoid assuming the husband committed haram.
D) Because the child is born after two years.

Key Takeaway
The reconciliation is valid if the wife gives birth within two years of divorce, because we presume intercourse before divorce using the principle that a Muslim does not commit haram.

Khalwa and Claim of No Intercourse: No Right to Return
Ruling with proof, objection, and response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a husband claims no intercourse after valid seclusion and explain why the right to return is denied based on the principle of admission.

Core Idea
A husband's admission of no intercourse after khalwa is binding against him, barring his right to return.

In this lesson, we examine a nuanced ruling from the chapter on divorce. The key is understanding that valid seclusion (khalwa sahiha) triggers full mahr but does not prove actual intercourse. The husband's own admission that he did not consummate is accepted, leading to a particular legal outcome. We will walk through the ruling, its proof, an objection, and the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Identify the scenario: husband has valid seclusion, claims no intercourse, then divorces. The ruling is that he does not have the right to take her back (ruj'ah).
The divorce of a non-consummated wife is ba'in without 'iddah.
2
Locate the Dalil
The proof is the principle of admission (iqrar): the husband's statement that he did not consummate is taken as true against his interest, so he is treated as a non-consummator.
Admission is a strong proof in Hanafi law.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
Since the right to return depends on consummation, and the husband admitted to no consummation, his right to return is nullified. The admission is legally effective.
The admission is accepted despite the obligation of full mahr.
4
Note the Objection
An objection states that full mahr is due, which is a sign of consummation, so the husband should be considered a liar and have the right to return.
This objection arises from a misunderstanding of khalwa's effect.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi Response
The response distinguishes: full mahr is due because of the handing over of the vulva (baz') in khalwa, not because of actual intercourse. Thus, the mahr obligation does not contradict the admission, and the ruling stands.
The contrast with pregnancy cases shows when admission is overridden.

Source Text

باب طلاق المريض

اب صورت مسئله یه ہے كه اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی کے ساتھ خلوت کی اور دروازه بند کر لیا یا پرده ڈال لیا اور کهنے لگا كه میں نے اس کے ساتھ جماع نهیں کیا ہے۔ پھر اس کو طلاق دے دی تو یه رجعت کا مالک نهیں هوگا۔ کیونکہ غیر مدخول به طلاق کے بعد بغیر عدت کے بائنه هو جاتی ہے اس وجه سے رجعت نهیں کر سکتا ۔

دلیل یه ہے كه ملک نکاح کا موکد هونا ولی سے هوتا ہے حالانکه وه ولی نه کرنے کا اقرار کر چکا ہے۔ پس اس کے حق میں اس کے اقرار کی تصدیق کی جائے گی اور رجعت اس کا حق ہے لهذار جمعت کا حق باطل کرنے میں اس کا قول معتبر هوگا۔ ولم يصر مكذبا شرعاً سے اعتراض کا جواب ہے۔ اعتراض یه ہے که شریعت کی جانب سے اس شخص کی بهی تکذیب کی گئی ہے۔ اس لئے که اس پر شریعت نے مهر کامل واجب کیا ہے اور مهر کامل طلاق بعد الدخول کی صورت میں واجب هوتا ہے نه که طلاق قبل الدخول کی صورت میں پس اس شخص پر مهر کامل کا واجب هونا اس بات کی دلیل ہے که شریعت نے اس کے قول لم اجا معها کا اعتبار نهیں کیا بلکه اس قول میں اس کی تکذیب کی ہے اور جب یه شخص اپنے اس قول میں شرعاً جھوٹا ہے تو گویا طلاق جماع کے بعد واقع کی گئی اور جماع کے بعد طلاق کی صورت میں رجعت کا اختیا ر رهتا ہے لهذا اس کو اس صورت میں رجعت کا اختیار هونا چاهے۔

جواب ... مهر سمی کا مؤکد هونا مبدل یعنی بضع کے سپرد کرنے پر موقوف ہے نه که قبضه (ولی) کرنے پر پس خلوت مجھے کے ذریعہ مهر سمی کا مؤکد هونا اس مرد کے واطی هونے پر دلالت نهیں کرتا۔ لهذا شوہر پر مهر کامل کے واجب هونے سے اس کا واطی هونا لازم نهیں آتا ۔ پس ثابت هو گیا که شریعت کی جانب سے اس کے قول لم اجامعها میں اس کی تکذیب نهیں کی گئی۔

اس کے برخلاف پہلی صورت که حمل اور ثبوت نسب بغیر ولی کے نهیں هو سکتا ہے۔ لهذا اس صورت میں اس کا مکذب هونا ثابت هو جائے گا۔
Chapter of Divorce of the Sick (Patient). Now the scenario is that if a husband secluded himself with his wife, locked the door or drew a curtain, and said, 'I did not have intercourse with her,' then gives her divorce, he will not have the right to take her back (رجعت). Because a non-consummated wife becomes ba'in after divorce without 'iddah, so he cannot return. The proof is that the strengthening of the marriage tie (ملك النكاح) is through consummation (ولاية), while he has admitted to not consummating. So his admission is accepted against his interest, and the right to return is his right, so his statement is considered valid to nullify the right of return. This is in response to the objection that he is not considered a liar in the eyes of Shari'ah. The objection is that Shari'ah has contradicted him, because it made full mahr obligatory on him, and full mahr is only obligatory in case of divorce after consummation, not before. So the obligation of full mahr indicates that Shari'ah did not accept his statement 'I did not have intercourse with her,' but rather contradicted him in that statement. And when he is a liar in Shari'ah in this statement, then the divorce is as if it occurred after intercourse, and after intercourse he retains the right of return, so he should have the right of return in this case. The response: The obligation of the specified mahr is dependent on the handing over of the object of exchange (the vulva), not on the act of possession (consummation). So the obligation of the specified mahr through valid seclusion (khalwa sahiha) does not indicate that he actually had intercourse. Therefore, the obligation of full mahr does not necessitate that he was an actual penetrator. Thus it is established that Shari'ah did not contradict him in his statement 'I did not have intercourse.' In contrast to the first scenario (where pregnancy and lineage cannot be without consummation), so in that case his being contradicted is established.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from the chapter on divorce of a sick person. It presents a scenario where a husband has valid seclusion (khalwa sahiha) with his wife, then claims he did not consummate, and subsequently divorces her. The ruling is that he does not have the right to take her back (رجعت) because, according to his own admission, she is non-consummated (ghayr madkhul biha), and divorce of a non-consummated wife is ba'in without 'iddah, so no return is possible. The proof is based on the principle that a person's admission is taken against him: his admission that he did not consummate is accepted, and since the right to return is established only through consummation, his admission nullifies that right. The objection raised claims that Shari'ah contradicts him because full mahr is obligatory, which is a sign of consummation. The Hanafi response explains that the obligation of full mahr in khalwa sahiha is based on the handing over of the vulva (بضع), not actual intercourse, so it does not prove he lied. Thus his statement stands, and the right to return is denied. The passage then contrasts with a case where pregnancy or established lineage would prove intercourse, making him a liar. The authority weight is from Zahir al-Riwayah as transmitted in Al-Mabsut and Al-Jami' al-Saghir, with the Mabsut narration considered more correct.

Key Terms
خلوت صحیح (khalwa sahiha): Valid seclusion between a husband and wife that is free from any impediment, after which the wife is considered as if consummated for certain legal rulings (e.g., full mahr) but not for actual intercourse.
رجعت (ruj'ah): The right of a husband to take back his divorced wife within her 'iddah period without a new marriage contract.
مهر کامل (mahr kamil): The full dower that becomes obligatory upon valid consummation or valid seclusion (khalwa sahiha) in Hanafi law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the husband lose the right to return after divorce if he claims no intercourse during valid seclusion?
A) Because the wife becomes ba'in immediately and cannot be taken back
B) Because his admission of no intercourse is accepted, proving he never consummated
C) Because the obligation of full mahr indicates he consummated
D) Because he lied about the seclusion

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between the effect of khalwa on mahr and on actual intercourse; the husband's admission of no intercourse bars his right to return.

Validity of Return (رجعت) When Wife Gives Birth Within Two Years
Explains a scenario where after khalwa and claim of no intercourse, a later return is validated by childbirth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a husband's return (رجعت) after divorce when the wife gives birth within two years, and explain the legal proof using the principle of haml fi‘l al-muslim ‘ala al-salah.

Core Idea
If a divorced wife gives birth within two years, the husband's return is valid even if he claimed no intercourse.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in Hanafi family law: a husband divorces his wife after valid seclusion, claims he never had intercourse, and then returns to her (رجعت). Later, she gives birth within two years from the divorce. The ruling stated in Al-Hidayah is that the return is valid. The reasoning relies on the child's lineage as proof of consummation and the principle of attributing a Muslim's action to righteousness. Understanding this ruling clarifies the interplay of evidence and presumption in marital matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and the Contested Element
Focus on the husband's claim of no intercourse and the later birth. The core question is whether the return can be valid despite the denial.
The husband's claim conflicts with the birth, creating an evidentiary puzzle.
2
State the Hanafi Ruling
The return (رجعت) is valid (sahih) if the wife gives birth within two years minus one day from the date of divorce. The two-year period is counted from divorce, not from the return.
This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Locate the Proof (Dalil)
The proof is rational (dalil aqli): the child's lineage is established from the husband, proving that intercourse occurred. Since the wife did not admit to completing 'iddah, and the pregnancy could have started before divorce, the law presumes intercourse happened before divorce.
The child's lineage is a decisive evidence in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Understand the Principle Applied
The principle of 'حمل فعل المسلم على الصلاح' (carrying a Muslim's action on righteousness) is used to choose the lawful interpretation: attributing intercourse before divorce avoids making it forbidden (since post-divorce intercourse would be haram as she would be ba'in).
This principle prioritizes lawful over unlawful interpretations of ambiguous actions.
5
Note the Time Limit
The maximum pregnancy duration in Hanafi school is two years. If the child is born after two years, the lineage is not established, and the return would be invalid.
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) have shorter maximum durations, leading to different rulings in some cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرماتے نهیں که اگر خلوت صحیح کے بعد یه کهه کر که میں نے جماع نهیں کیا اپنی اس بیوی سے مراجعت کر لی پھر اس عورت نے دو سال سے ایک روز کم میں بچه جنا تو یه رجعت صحیح هوگئی، دو سال کا اعتبار یوم طلاق سے هوگا نه که یوم رجعت سے، رجعت کے درست هونے پر دلیل یه ہے که اس بچه کا نسب اس سے ثابت هوگا نه که دوسرے نے ۔ کیونکہ عورت نے عدت کے گزرنے کا اقرار نهیں کیا ہے اور بچه دو سال تک ماں کے پیٹ میں باقی ره سکتا ہے۔ پس نسب کا ثابت هونا اس بات کی دلیل ہے که یه شخص جس نے لم اجا معها کہا ہے اپنی بیوی سے وطی کر چکا ہے۔ اب رهی یه بات که یه ولی طلاق سے پہلے ہے یا بعد میں تو اگر چه اتمال دونوں میں گر ہم اسکو ولی قبل الطلاق پر محمول کریں گے۔ اس لئے اگر ولی بعد الطلاق پر محمول کریں تو یه ولی حرام هوگی۔ کیونکہ جب طلاق سے پہلے ولی نهیں پائی گئی تو یه عورت بغیر عدت کے نفس طلاق سے بائنه هو جائے گی اور اس کے بعد وطی کرنا حرام هوگا اور مسلمان فعل حرام کا ارتکاب نهیں کرتا ۔ اس وجه سے اس وطی کو قبل الطلاق پر محمول کریں گے تا که مسلمان کے فعل کو صلاح پر محمول کیا جاسکے۔
They say: If after valid seclusion, saying 'I did not have intercourse,' he returned to this wife, then she gave birth in less than two years minus one day, then this return is valid. The consideration of two years will be from the day of divorce, not from the day of return. The proof for the validity of the return is that the child's lineage will be established from him, not from another. Because the woman did not admit to the passage of 'iddah, and the child can remain in the mother's womb for up to two years. So the establishment of lineage is proof that this man, who said 'I did not have intercourse,' indeed had intercourse with his wife. Now as to whether this intercourse was before the divorce or after, although it is possible in both ways, we attribute it to intercourse before the divorce. Because if we attribute it to after the divorce, then this intercourse would be forbidden. When intercourse before divorce was not found (admitted), this woman would become ba'in by the very divorce without 'iddah, and intercourse after that would be forbidden. And a Muslim does not commit a forbidden act. Therefore, we attribute this intercourse to before the divorce so that the Muslim's action can be attributed to righteousness.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling within the same chapter, building on the previous scenario. It discusses a case where a husband, after khalwa sahiha and claiming no intercourse, returns to his wife (رجعت), and then she gives birth within two years (minus one day) from the divorce. The ruling is that this return is valid, even though the husband claimed no prior intercourse. The proof is that the child's lineage is established to him, which proves intercourse occurred. Since the wife does not admit to completing 'iddah, and the child could be from before the divorce, and because attributing intercourse to after the divorce would make it forbidden (as she would be ba'in), the law presumes the intercourse was before the divorce, thus validating the return. This is based on the principle of carrying a Muslim's action on the best interpretation (حمل فعل المسلم على الصلاح). The authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
حمل فعل المسلم على الصلاح (haml fi‘l al-muslim ‘ala al-salah): A legal principle that when a Muslim's action admits of both a lawful and unlawful interpretation, it is attributed to the lawful one.
عدت ('iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarrying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the husband's return considered valid when his divorced wife gives birth within two years?
A) Because he admitted to intercourse after the return
B) Because the child's lineage proves consummation before divorce, so the return is valid
C) Because the wife's 'iddah had ended
D) Because he divorced her before seclusion

Key Takeaway
A husband's return to his divorced wife is valid if she gives birth within two years, because the child's lineage proves consummation and Muslim actions are presumed lawful.

Second Childbirth as Return (رجعة) in Conditional Divorce
Ruling on second childbirth as return after conditional divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a second childbirth constitutes a return in a conditional divorce and apply the ruling to a given scenario.

Core Idea
A second child born at least six months after the first establishes the husband's return during iddah, even years later.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi family law: a husband suspends divorce on his wife's childbirth. When the first child is born, divorce takes effect, and the wife enters iddah. If she then gives birth to a second child, the question arises: does this second birth constitute a return (رجعة) by the husband? The Hanafi school answers yes, provided the births are separated by at least six months, indicating a distinct pregnancy that began during the iddah. This ruling is grounded in the presumption that intercourse occurred during iddah, and that the wife has not admitted the conclusion of her iddah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional divorce formulation
Note the husband says 'If you give birth, you are divorced.' This is a suspended (muallaq) divorce that takes effect upon the first birth.
The condition is childbirth (wilaadah), not the number of children.
2
Determine the legal consequence of the first birth
Upon the first birth, three things happen: (1) the divorce becomes effective, (2) the wife begins her iddah, (3) the husband still has the right to return during the iddah.
Iddah for a pregnant woman lasts until delivery, but here the divorce occurs at first birth, so iddah begins then.
3
Analyze the second birth as evidence of intercourse during iddah
If the second child is born at least six months after the first, it indicates a separate pregnancy that must have begun after the first birth. Since the wife was in iddah during that time, the pregnancy can only be attributed to intercourse by the husband, which constitutes a return.
The six-month gap is the minimum period for a viable pregnancy to produce a child.
4
Consider the role of the wife's acknowledgment
The wife's failure to admit the end of iddah means the iddah is presumed ongoing. If she had admitted completion, the second child would not automatically be a return.
The ruling presumes that intercourse during iddah is a return, regardless of the husband's intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه لپس اگر مرد نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا اذا ولدت فانت طالق ۔ پھر اس نے بچه جنا پھر دوسرا بچه لائی تو یه ولادت ثانیه رجعت ہے۔ اس کی مراد یه ہے که ( دوسرا بچه ) طن آخر سے هو اور بطن آخر یه ہے که چه ماہ کے بعد هو ۔ اگر چه دو سال سے زیاده هو جب تک که عورت نے عدت گزرنے کا اقرار نهیں کیا ہے۔ کیونکہ اس پر دلد اول سے طلاق واقع هوگئی اور عدت واجب هو گئی ۔ پس ولید ثانی ایسے علوق سے هو گا جو شوہر سے عورت کی عدت میں پیدا هوا۔ کیونکہ عورت نے عدت گزرنے کا اقرار نهیں کیا ہے۔ پس وه (شومر ) رجعت کرنے والا هوگا۔ تشریح مسئله یه ہے که اگر کسی مرد نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا ذا ولدت فانت طالق ۔ پس اس نے ایک بچه پیدا هوا۔ پھر دوسرا بچه چه ماہ کے بعد پیدا هوا تو یه دوسرے بچه کا پیدا هوتا رجعت ہے۔ حاصل یه که دونوں بچوں کے درمیان چه ماہ کا فاصله ہے برابر ہے که ولادت ثانیه دو سال سے کم میں هو یا دو سال سے زیاده میں دونوں صورتوں میں رجعت ثابت هو جائے گی۔ دلیل یه ہے که پہلے بچه کی ولادت سے اس عورت پر طلاق هوی اور عدت واجب هوگئی ۔ پس دوسرے بچه کی ولادت کے بارے میں کها جائے گا که شوہر نے عدت کے زمانے میں اس عورت سے جماع کیا تھا اس کے نتیجے میں یه دوسرا بچه پیدا هوا ہے اور عورت نے عدت گزرنے کا اقرار بهی نهیں کیا ہے ۔ پس یه شخص مطلقه رعیه کے ساتھ اس کی عدت میں جماع کرنے کی وجه سے رجعت کرنے والا شمار هوگا ۔
Translation: If a man said to his wife, 'When you give birth, you are divorced.' Then she gave birth to a child, then she brought a second child, then this second birth is a return (رجعت). Its meaning is that the second child is from a different womb (بطن آخر), and a different womb means after six months. Even if it is more than two years, as long as the woman did not admit to the passing of the 'iddah. Because upon the first child, divorce occurred and 'iddah became obligatory. So the second child will be from a pregnancy (علوق) that occurred from the husband during the wife's 'iddah. Because the woman did not admit to the passing of the 'iddah. So he (the man) is considered as one who returns. Commentary: The scenario is that if a man said to his wife 'When you give birth, you are divorced.' Then a child was born, then a second child after six months, then the birth of this second child is a return. The conclusion is that the interval between the two children is six months, whether the second birth is within two years or more, in both cases the return is established. The proof is that the first child's birth effected divorce and 'iddah became obligatory. Then regarding the second child's birth, it is said that the husband had intercourse with this woman during the 'iddah period, resulting in this second child, and the woman did not admit to the passing of the 'iddah. So this person, because of intercourse with his divorced wife during her 'iddah, is considered as one who returns (رجعت).
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This section discusses a conditional divorce suspended on childbirth. The condition is 'اذا ولدت فانت طالق' (if you give birth, you are divorced). When the first child is born, the divorce takes effect, and the wife begins her 'iddah. If she then gives birth to a second child, provided the interval between births is at least six months (indicating a separate pregnancy), this second birth is considered a return (رجعة) by the husband, because the intercourse that led to the second pregnancy occurred during the 'iddah. The proof is that since the wife does not acknowledge the completion of 'iddah, and the child can be born up to two years from the divorce, the second pregnancy is attributed to intercourse during 'iddah, which constitutes a valid return. This ruling applies whether the second birth is within two years or beyond, as long as the wife has not admitted the end of 'iddah. The key condition is a gap of at least six months between births, indicating a new pregnancy. This is based on the Hanafi position that a pregnancy can last up to two years.

Key Terms
تعلیق الطلاق (ta‘liq al-talaq): Suspending the occurrence of divorce on the fulfillment of a future condition, such as childbirth.
بطن (batn): A pregnancy or a delivery episode. In this context, 'different batn' means a separate pregnancy, indicated by at least six months between births.
رجعة (raj‘ah): Return of the husband to his wife during the iddah period, by which the marriage is reinstated without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Whenever the second child is born with a gap of at least six months, as long as 'iddah has not been admitted to end
D) Only if the second child is born within two years of the divorce

Key Takeaway
A second child born at least six months after the first, with no admission of iddah completion, is proof of the husband's return during iddah, regardless of the time elapsed.

Conditional Divorce with 'Whenever You Give Birth': Three Children in Separate Pregnancies
Ruling for repeated conditional divorce using 'klm' and three children in separate pregnancies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a conditional divorce using 'klm' when three children are born from separate pregnancies, and explain the interplay between return (رجعة) and the repeated condition.

Core Idea
With 'klm', each birth is both a return and a new divorce — the condition repeats the consequence.

In the previous lesson, we saw how a single conditional divorce with birth during 'iddah leads to a revocation. Now the text expands this to a repeated condition using 'klm'. This creates a cycle where each new birth revives the marriage (as a return) but also immediately terminates it again. Understanding this interplay is crucial for correctly counting divorces in multiple pregnancies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wording and its implication
'klm' (كلما) means 'whenever' or 'every time'. In Hanafi law, it makes the condition repetitive: each occurrence of the condition (birth of a child) triggers the consequence (divorce) again.
This is distinct from a single conditional word like 'idha' (if).
2
Recognize the sequence of events
The wife gives birth to three children in separate pregnancies (بطون مختلفة). Each pregnancy is separate, meaning at least six months between births.
Separate pregnancies are relevant because the 'iddah from the previous birth ends with the next birth.
3
Apply the principle of return via intercourse during 'iddah
Intercourse during 'iddah counts as a return (رجعة). When the second child is born, it is conceived during the first 'iddah, so the birth is a return. Similarly, the third child is conceived during the second 'iddah, so its birth is also a return.
The return occurs automatically upon the birth (the marriage is considered revived from the time of conception).
4
Combine the condition and the return
Each birth is both a return (from the previous 'iddah) and a new occurrence of the condition. Because of 'klm', each birth also triggers a new divorce immediately after the return.
Thus the second and third births are simultaneous acts of return and divorce.
5
Count the divorces
First birth: first divorce, 'iddah begins. Second birth: return then second divorce, new 'iddah. Third birth: return then third divorce. After third divorce, she is irrevocably divorced (ba'in). Thereafter, 'iddah is by menstrual cycles (أقراء).
No further returns or divorces because the marriage is extinguished.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان قال كلما ولدت ولدا فانت طالق فولدت ثلثة أولاد فى بطون مختلفة فالولد الاول طلاق والولد الثاني رجعة وكذا الثالث لانها اذاجاءت بالولد الأول وقع الطلاق وصارت معتدة وبالثاني صار مراجعا لما بينا انه يجعل العلوق بوطى حادث في العدة ويقع الطلاق الثانى بولادة الولد الثانى لان اليمين معقودة بكلمة كلما ووجبت العدة وبالولد الثالث صار مراجعا لما ذكرنا وتقع الطلقة الثالثة بولادة الثالث ووجبت العدة بالاقراء لانها حامل من ذوات الحيض حين وقع الطلاق
And if he said, 'Whenever you give birth to a child, you are divorced,' so she gave birth to three children in different pregnancies (بطون مختلفة). So the first child is divorce, the second child is return (رجعة), and likewise the third. Because when she came with the first child, divorce occurred and she became a woman in 'iddah; with the second child, he becomes a returner (مراجع), according to what we explained: that the pregnancy is considered from intercourse that occurred during the 'iddah. And the second divorce falls by the birth of the second child, because the oath is bound by the word 'كلما' (whenever). And 'iddah becomes obligatory. And with the third child, he becomes a returner according to what we mentioned. And the third divorce falls by the birth of the third child. And 'iddah becomes obligatory by the menstrual periods (أقراء) because she is a woman of menstruation at the time of divorce.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage expands on the previous ruling to a case where the husband uses the word 'كلما' (whenever) in the conditional divorce. The phrase 'كلما ولدت ولدا فانت طالق' means that each time she gives birth to a child, she is divorced. In this scenario, she has three children from three separate pregnancies (each separated by at least six months). The ruling: the first child's birth triggers the first divorce, and she enters 'iddah. The second child is born from a pregnancy conceived during that 'iddah, so the birth of the second child constitutes a return (رجعة) by the husband due to intercourse during 'iddah. However, because the condition is repeated (كلما), the birth of the second child also triggers the second divorce ('you are divorced' again). Then she enters a new 'iddah. The third child is also conceived during the second 'iddah, so its birth is a return (رجعة) but also triggers the third and final divorce. After the third divorce, she is irrevocably separate (ba'in), and her 'iddah is by menstrual cycles (أقراء) because she is a menstruating woman at the time of that divorce. The logic follows the same principle: every pregnancy from intercourse during 'iddah is a return, but the conditional wording makes each birth a new divorce. This demonstrates the interplay between conditional divorce and return through childbirth.

Key Terms
كلما (kullamā): A conditional word meaning 'whenever' or 'every time', used in suspending divorce on recurring events.
بطون مختلفة (butūn mukhtalifah): Different pregnancies; each pregnancy is considered separate when there is a gap of at least six months.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only one divorce
B) Two divorces
C) Three divorces
D) No divorce because the condition is invalid

Key Takeaway
In a conditional divorce using 'klm', each birth from a separate pregnancy triggers a divorce, and intermediate births also serve as returns.
